CHADWICK
DENOUNCED
BY HIS WIFE
Woman Gives Free
â¢ Rein to Her
Rage,
Says Her Husband Had No
Money Except Wbat
She Gave Him.
'Til Suffer, but Not Alone, Mark You,"
Her Warning to Those Who
Turned Against Her.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
CLEVELAND, Jan. B.â"Fear for
gery charges?" exclaimed Mrs. Cassie
L. Chadwick to a Philadelphia North
American reporter in jail here to-day.
"Neverl They are false. I fear neither
them nor the technical charges in the
Federal courts. I'm neither afraid of
this court nor any court in the land.
1 defy them all.
"Men are cowards. They turn their
backs on a woman when she is down.
They were once so full of assurances,
and - proffers of aid. Now where are
they? But they shall pay for it! I'll
suffer, but not alone, mark you. They
Shall pay their share.
"Dr. Chadwick knew me for three
years before we were married in Pitts
burg. Now, if he didn't find out who
I was in all that time, it isn't my fault,
is it? Am I to blame If he didn't
know whom he was marrying?
"You musn't think that Dr. Chad
wick lost his fortune through my
transactions," she said. "That isn't
true. In the first place, he never had
a private fortune to lose, and in the
second place I gave him more than he
ever had before. This talk of his los
ing $70,000 of his own money through
me is all rot. He never had that much
money.
"The doctor's present impoverished
condition appeals to the sympathy of
his friends. His return home in the
second cabin on a comparatively cheap
boat and his habit of eating at cheap
restaurants mean nothing. He always
did travel that way, except when I was
paying the bills.
"Why, if Dr. Chadwick had $5000
In his pocket and nothing to do with
a penny of it he would rather walk
fifteen miles than spend a nickel for
car fare. That's the kind of man he
Is, and you know it."
Commenting on the fact that he had
been served with an order by the Fed
eral authorities to "produce certain
diamonds and gems," At.torney J. P.
Dawley, counsel for Mrs. Cassie L.
Chadwick, said to-day:
"I have not received so much as a
pewter shoe buckle from either Mrs.
â¢Chadwick or her husband. The serv
ing of such an order strikes me as be
ing humorous."
BUFFALO, N. T., Jan. B.âNathan
liOeser of Cleveland, receiver of the es
tate of Mrs. Chadwick, was in Buffalo
to-day on his way home from New
York, where he had been making an
Investigation :n connection with the
Chadwick estates. Loeser said that
)ie feared that the amount of assets
of the estate so far in hand had been
grossly exaggerated.
"The property which I have in my
possession now, including the house,
freal estate and household effects,
cught, if sold, to bring about $100,000.
There is a real estate mortgage of $35,
--000 on that and a chattel mortgage of
17000," he said.

Japan Trade Falling Off.
VANCOUVER, B. C, Jan. B.âSta
tistics regarding trade between Can
ada and Japan show a decline during
the past year. The value of Canadian
goods exported to Japan and of Jap
anese goods brought into Canada was
considerably less in 1904 than in 1903.

EARL SAFE
WITH PARTY
AT PANAMA
Fear Concerning
Fitzwilliam Is
Allayed.
Special Cable to The Call and New York
Herald. Copyright, 1905, by the New York
Herald Publishing Company.
LONDON, Jan. B.âAnxiety as to the
safety of the steamship Veronique and
her crew was allayed to-day. when
Cowper, Lord Fitzwilliam's private sec
retary, received a cablegram from Pan
ama, announcing that the Veronique
was undamaged, and that captain, offi
cers and crew were well. The whole
party has left Colon by steamship, and
will arrive at Southampton within the
next three weeks.
A dispatch from London yesterday
stated that the Veronique had met with
an accident, but gave no details. On
board the vessel is Earl Fitzwilliam's
expedition, which had planned to seek
for buried treasure in an island in the
South Pacific. In the party are several
distinguished Englishmen.
MILWAUKEE
HAS HORSE
THAT ADDS
Rival ol "Hans," the
German Equine
Wonder.
*
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MILWAUKEE. Jan. 8.â"Colonel
Dixon" Is only a delivery wagon horse,
but the animal is learning tricks so
fast under the training of his driver,
Isaac Goodman, that his owner. James
Conroy, is seriously considering placing
him on exhibition. Colonel Dixon is
five years old. He first began to show
signs of unusual intelligence a year
ago.
"At that time I used to teach him to
go to customers' houses without driv
ing," said Goodman. "Dixon seemed to
understand perfectly. 'Go to Bacon's.'
I would say. Away would go Dixon,
turning the corners, and finally stop
ping at Bacon's. 'Perele's next.' and at
Perele's he would stop. In this way
Dixon was able to go to a dozen or
rriore places without being driven."
The horse already can count.
"Colonel Dixon, take three from five
âcan you do it?" asked Goodman of the
horse.
Colonel Dixon nodded his head. Then
he gave answer by stamping his foot
two times.
"Add four and three," said Good
man.
Colonel Dixon promptly stamped his
foot seven times.
Goodman attributes the intelligence
of the animal to telepathy.
"I believe the horse is hypnotized."
said he. "I believe It is telepathy that
makes It possible for the anlmai to srive
correct answers. I aslt him to add three
and four, and I have the answer, seven,
firmly in my mind. I believe the flsrure
is then transmitted to the animal and
he gives the answer. I have never
heard of telepathy being applied to an
imals, but this is the only way in which
I can account for Colonel Dixon's
tricks. I have recently read all about
the tricks of Hans, the German horse,
and I believe before I get through with I
Dixon I can make him do the' same."

WILL OPEN
HOTEL FOR
WORKMEN
New Philanthropy Is
Planned hy John
Arbuckle.
ââÂ» â
Well-Equipped Hostelry to
Charge Guests Filty
Cents a Day.
âÂ»
Will Be the First of a Series to
Be Established in New
York City.
Special Dispatch to The CML
NEW Jan. B.âAn advertise
ment which tells of clean, well ven
tilated rooms; healthful, substantial,
well-cooked food, with books, maga
zines and a piano in a common living
room in an especially erected building
in the heart of New York, at 50 cents a
day for men and 40 cents for women,
reveals the latest philanthropic plan
of John Arbuckle, the coffee and sugar
merchant, who established the "floating
hotels" for poor people.
While the attractiveness of the home
he proposes to open will appeal to the
more prosperous classes than those
which it is designed to aid, Arbuckle
intends to exclude all but wage-earners
of both sexes and of good character.
He will in person supervise his novel
hotel. Where this marvel of comfort
and modest rates in boarding-houses is
located Arbuckle declines as yet to dis
close. He said to-day:
"The building is not yet completed,
and will not be for two weeks or more.
I advertised as an experiment largely
and to see how many persons would
apply. When I get answers I will go
over them all carefully and select from
the applicants those who appear to be
the right kind. To each of these I shall
send a pamphlet descriptive of the
hotel. It will accommodate only 100
guests, and at first I shall not let any
one engage a room there except by the
day.
"This hotel is not intended for de
pendents or invalid or aged persons. I
want to fill the house with young men
and women who are working for a liv
ing and as a rule cannot get for what
they can afford to pay anything but
the bare necessities of life. I want to
give at least 100 of them more than the
bare necessities."
John Arbuckle is a bachelor, more
than 60 years old. He and his brother
Charles came to New York and began
the sale of ground coffee in packages.
They went from this to refining sugar,
and aroused the antagonism of the
sugar trust. The "floating hotels"
which Arbuckle sends out on sea trips
every summer crowded with poor wom
en and children are his pet charity.
It is said that Arbuckle's new hotel,
should it prove the success expected,
will be only the first of a series to be
established.
CALIFORNIA
POTATOES
IN IRELAND
Tubers From This
. State Flourish
in Cork.
REDDING, Jan. B.âW. J. B. Martin,
a Pacheco district farmer, is in receipt
of a letter from R. H. Hayes, a Justice
of the Peace In County Cork, Ireland,
announcing that the small lot of Bur
bank potatoes sent to Hayes by Martin
one year ago have done famously,
even' eye in the lot escaping the po
tato blight.
Hayes, who is also secretary of the
County Cork Agricultural Society,
writes that he is so much encouraged
at the prospect of getting a blight
proof tuber from Pacheco district stock
that he has already decided on a name
for , the importationâ"Mogalore," a
Celtic word meaning "good enough."
Irish farmers have paid thousands of
pounds for alleged blight-proof pota
toes, but so far without avail.
Martin was in hopes of being instru
mental in having his birthplace.
County Cork, seeded to alfalfa, but
Hayes writes that the alraifa seed lent
him by Martin did not turn out so well
as the potatoes.

Special Dispatch to Th* Call.
NEW YORK, -Jan. B.âlncidents fol
lowed one another quickly to-day in
the affairs of Brodie L. Duke, the mil
lionaire tobacco man, who has been
committed to a sanitarium. They cul
minated to-night in a siege of the
house of George H. Mallory, Mrs.
Duke's attorney, by a woman who said
she was Mrs. Duke. Mallory refused
to have her admitted and for five min
utes she tried to gain an entrance.
B. L. Duke has been summoned to
appear before the Grand Jury to-mor
row. -Mrs. Duke will become a promi
nent figure in the investigation by the
Grand Jury andthe District Attorney
of the financial'affairs of her hus
band, with which she seems to have
be en ' intimately connected. Previous
to her marriage to Duke, three weeks
ago, Alice Webb had promoted com
panies, being a member of a stock
brokerage house, and had apparently
won the confidence of many bankers in
small towns of the South and Middle
Wtst.
The authorities at Bellevue Hospital
have deposited with the District Attor
ney the bonds, stocks, notes and
checks to the face value of about
$60,000, which were found in the
pockets of Duke when he was taken
into custody at the instance of hie
relatives on the ground that he was
not competent to manage his own af
fair*. '
Mrs. Agnes d'Esplaines, an associate
of Mrs. Duke, also has been sum
moned to appear before the Grand
Jury.
All persons connected with the af
fair were reticent to-day as to details,
but the hint was given in many quar
ters that developments of the great
est interest might be expected.
Concerning the financial career of
Miss Webb in Chicago and this city
many interesting facts developed to
day. Her connection with Mrs.
d'Esplaines seems to have had no con
nection with the various commercial
interests in which she was interested.
Mrs. d'Esplaines, who is her senior by
several years,, was once proprietress of
a resort on West Thirty-second street.
The two women have been on terms of
intimate friendship for many years
and they were living together when
M!ss Webb made the acquaintance of
Duke.
Dispatches from Chicago say that
Mrs. Duke is a daughter of W. H.
Webb of that city and that she in
herited her talent for financial man
agement from her father.
Rev. W. Woe, Dr. Parkhurst's
fist assistant, who performed the wed
ding ceremony for Duke and Miss
Webb, said to-night that he did so
knowing nothing of Miss Webb's ante
cedents and making no Inquiries about
them. He said also that at the time
Duke manifested no signs of mental
disturbance, although the clergyman
admitted that in the excitement of the
ceremony Duke gave answers In the
wrong place at times.
The statement was Â«made to-night
that George H. Mallory, Mrs. Duke's
attorney, had severed his relations with
the woman, and that this was the rea
son for her attempt to gain admission
to his residence.
Mallory was averse to entering into
any discussion of the transactions.
There are among the papers in the
temporary custody of the District At
torney's office three promissory notes
for $5000 each, said to have been made
on December 5 last by Duke to Miss
Webb, four days before their marriage,
and due in three, four and five months.
Mallory said he had an idea they were
to be used in connection with the
financing of the Texas-Cuba Tobacco
Company, of which Mrs. Duke was
president before her marriage.
It was said to-night that the decision
of the- Duke family to take steps to
annul the millionaire's marriage was
reached when it was discovered that
Mrs. Duke was once proprietress of the
"House of All Nations," a notorious
resort.
CHICAGO, Jan. B.âln marrying

steamsip Keemun, which arrived to

YOUTHFUL MURDERERS
MAKE A CONFESSION
Two Tramps Tell of Their Brutal Crime
TWO YOUNG TRAMPS WHO HAVE CONFESSED TO THE BRUTAL MURDER OF GEORGE DUNN, NEAR CRESCENT CITY
LAST MONTH. THEIR VICTIM AND THE SHERIFF OF DEL NORTE COUNTY. TO WHOM THE YOUTHFUL MURDER
ERS TOLD THE STORY OF THEIR HEINOUS CRIME.
Horrible Details of the Killing of Aged George Dunn Near
Crescent City Given to Sheriff of Del Norte.
CRESCENT CITY, Jan. B.âWithout
a tremor and with no display of
fear or sorrow, Harry Brown
and Frank Kelley. two youthful
"hoboes," now confined in the
Crescent City Jail, confessed that on
the night of December 29 they took
the life of George Dunn, a genial
Irishman, long past the three-score-
and-ten mark, who for years past
kept a store on the lonely Crescent
City-Grants Pass stage road. The con
fession was first wrung from Brown
by Sheriff Crawford, who, by cleverly
playing upon his fears that his part
ner in crime was about to turn state's
evidence, induced the youthful but
hardened murderer to tell the story
of the awful crime.
Glibly and with an air of bravado,
and still with the cringing and fawn
ing air of an Informer, Brown de
scribed the last moments of the aged
storekeeper, whose great heartedness
had won for him the respect and es
teem 6f the residents of Del Norte
County. - Only once did Brown falter
in his bloody tale, and then his hesi
tation was not due' to any feeling for
the white-haired old man whose life
he had helped to take. He faltered
because he did not know for a cer
tainty whether his confession would
earn him the reward of the cowardly
informer âusually immunity from the
death penalty. He hesitated long
enough to ask if he or his partner in
crime was to be benefited by the con
fession. When assured by the Sheriff
that he would "get all that was com
ing to him" he breathed a sigh of re
lief and went on with his tale of cow
ardly, treacherous murder.
PLANNED IN ADVANCE.
â¢ Armed with the facts obtained from
Brown, part of whose conversation
with the Sheriff had been overheard
by Kelley, purposely placed in an ad
joining cell, the official paid a visit to
the latter. He found him pacing his
cell in a frenzy of excitement, fearful
of the consequences because his com
panion in crime had been more craven
hearted and selfish than himself and
had forestalled him in his plan to save
his own neck at the expense of his
companion's.
"What's the matter with that fel
low?" he cried almost before the
Sheriff had entered the cell. "Is he
getting cold feet? Is he turning
state's evidence?"
The Sheriff, by evasive answers,
succeeded In still further arousing the
fears of Kelley. Possessing the craven
heart so essential to the makeup of
one who could take the life of an aged
and helpless man, Kelley could not
stand the strain. Cursing and swear
ing he paced his cell, occasionally
stopping to shake his fist in the direc
tion of his companion in crime. The
strain became too great for him to
Special Dispatch to The Call.
bear, and finally with a foul impreca
tion he said, "Bring me an attorney
and I will tell the whole story."
Quickly the Sheriff summoned Dis
trict Attorney F. W. Taft, and within
a short time Kelley gave to the two
officers the story of the brutal murder
of George Dunn at the hands of him
self and Brown. Not a detail was for
gotten by the youthful criminal. His
story was- even more complete than
that told the Sheriff by Brown.
Dunn's murder, according to the
tales of both men, was planned in
Waldo, or., two days before it was
committed. Forced to leave Waldo by
the officers of that town, Kelley and
Brown wended their way to California.
Their objective point was Dunn's
lonely roadhouse. From Brown first
came the suggestion of visiting the
old man. He had heard rumors that
the old fellow had gold galore hidden
in the store. The suggestion fell upon
willing ears. Kelley was as anxious
as Brown to get the money, at what
cost It did not matter. They stopped
at the Monumental mine and there
the club with which the old man's life
was beaten out was picked up. The
hands of Brown fashioned the weapon.
USE CLUB AND AN AX.
The two men knew that Dunn was
a courageous old fellow and Would
give battle If provoked. So they de
cided that Kelley should insult him,
while Brown would await a chance to
deal him a cowardly blow. But their
plans miscarried. At Dunn's they met
two men in search of work. The pres%
ence of the strangers delayed their
game, but they got rid of them by
sending them to the Monumental mine,
hastening their progress by telling
them that two men were needed there.
The strangers disposed of, Kelley
rapped at the door of Dunn. The store
keeper told him to enter. Upon enter
ing the store Kelley, deterred at first by
the stalwart figure of the Irishman,
lost heart and hesitated. A gesture
from Brown stirred him to action, and
he raised his hand and shoved the old
man against the counter.
"That's your game, is it?" said Dunn,
regaining his balance and facing his
assailant.
The old man bravely rushed at Kel
ley, who Jumped to one side. Brown
took a step forward and brought his
heavy club down upon the white head
of the gallant old man, felling him to
the floor. The old man struggled *o
rise, but another blow stretched him
senseless upon the floor. Again and
again did the club fall, and soon life
was extinct.
"Get an ax," cried Brown to Kelley.
The latter did as he was told, and
when he returned Brown compelled him
to use the weapon upon the prostrate
form.
"I did not hit him hard," whlnlngly
said Kelley during his confession.

CONNER SVILLE. Ind., Jan. i.â
There is trouble and work ahead for
the swains of Connersvllle. In fact. If
the plans of twenty-five young women
here, who have been horrified by fre
quent divorce suits, do not miscarry,
there will be more "giving the mitten"
than giving in marriage for some time.
So deeply moved are these women that
they have organized themselves Into
a committee of the whole to investi

any member.
As in the most exclusive of clubs, a
blackball cast by a single member of
the organization will be sufficient to
bar the would-be benedict
While love stricken men do not take
such a horrid view of the* situation,
they are none the less dismayed at the
expensive prospect, to say nothing of
the difficulty that may be expected in
seeking the approval â¢! twenty-five

CAPTURED RUSSIAN OFFICERS PREFER PRISON
LIFE WITH THEIR IN TO BEING PAROLED
FIFTY PER CENT DECLINE THEIR FREEDOM
Transfer of Port
Arthur Army
Completed.
TOKIO, January B.âGeneral Nogi's
report,- received at 11 o'clock last I
night, announces that the transfer of j
Port Arthur prisoners was completed j
on Saturday afternoon. The total .
number was 24,369 â878 officers and |
23,491 men. \
Fifty per cent of the officers will
share imprisonment with their men.
The Japanese intend to establish a
naval station at Port Arthur. Vice-
Admiral Y. Shibayama will be placed
in charge. The military administra
tion at Port Arthur will retain only
a small garrison as soon as the pris
oners are withdrawn and order is
restored.
The fleet is busily engaged in clear
ing mines, but owing to their great
number navigation will be unsafe for
a long time. Only Government craft
will be allowed to enter the harbor.
It is probable that Dalny will soon
be opened to neutrals. Japanese com
panies are prenaring to establish
weekly services to that port.
It is proposed to float a fourth
domestic war loan soon, under the
same conditions as the third was
negotiated.
Lieutenants General Fock, Smyrnoff
and Gorbatowsky and Rear Admiral
Wilmann will be brought to Japan as
prisoners of war. Lieutenant General
Stoessel will leave Dalny on January
12th on a Japanese transport for Nag
asaki. He will be accompanied by
the other paroled officers. The Rus
sians probably will remain in Japan
a few days and then proceed on a
French steamship to Europe, going by
way of the Suez canal.
Reports previously received were to
the effect that both Lieutenant
General Fock and Lieutenant Gener
al Smyrnoff had bzen killed at Port
Arthur. On January 2 a St. Peters
burg dispatch denied that Fock had
been killed, ' ut admitted that he had
been wounded. A dispatch received
from Mukden on January 7 said
that while the death of Fock had not
been published, it had for six weeks
beÂ»r> accepted there as a fact. The
report of General Smyrnoff's death
had never been confirmed.
CUNNING OF STOESSEL.
Hospitals Placed So as to Protect All
Port Arthur.
â¢ LONDON, Jan. 9.âThe correspon
dent at Tokio of the Times says:
"The map of Port Arthur, which
the Russians produced on December
18 in support of their complaint that
the Japanese fire was damaging hos
pitals, shows seventeen hospitals scat
tered throughout the old and new
towns. To avoid hitting these the
Japanese would have had to refrain
from firing altogether in the direction
of both towns."
Japanese Attempt a Surprise.
PORT ARTHUR. Jan. B.âThe Rus
sian prisoners, while waiting to-day
at Changkinztzu, a railway station for
Dalny. celebrated the approach of
their New Year. Religious services
were held in the morning at 10 o'clock
and the assemblage of prisoners was
addressed by a Russiau priest in full
canonicals. After the services there
was music and dancing on the open
ground in front of the station.
The 8-year-old daughter of a Rus
sian officer was among the prison
ers. Fifty per cent of the officers are
going to Japan.
Opium smoking has reached im
mense proportions in the French ports
on the Mediterranean Sea. Laws have
been passed in Marseilles and Toulon
forbidding the "rolling of pills" in
public places in those cities.

In the Czar's Cap
ital Peace Talk
Is Heard.
ST. PETERSBURG, Jan. 9, 2:10 a. m.
The conference whicn Emperor
Nicholas has been holding with
members of the Council of the
Empire and other advisers on the
internal and external situation has
been concluded, but nothing has oc
curred which would indicate that the
Government is prepared to depart from
itp present programme of continuing
the war to the bitter end. Neverthe
less, talk of peace was heard on Sun
day in many quarters, the foundation
for it being ascribed to Foreign Min
ister Lamsdorff's alleged position in
favor of such a course, on the ground
that, aside from the humiliation to
military prestige involved, it would
only mean the relinquishment of Man
churia, to which Russia already is for
mally pledged.
There is no confirmation of this
statement of Count Lamsdorff's posi
tion, beyond the general knowledge
that he always has belonged to the 1
peace party. It is quite probable that
the peace talk has been produced by
the arrival of foreign papers, as it re
ceives no encouragement in responsi
ble quarters here.
The belief which existed in well-in
formed diplomatic circles that Japan
would follow the fall of Port Arthur
with moderate peace propositions has
not been realized. Up to the present
time nothing has reached the Govern
ment, either through the United States
or other channels. Diplomats here can
not figure out the general lines of a
treaty at this juncture which both bel
ligerents would accept, but realize that
if Japan makes any sort of offer, un
less it is rejected outright as not en
tertainable, negotiations would be
opened, and. negotiations once entered
upon, friends of both parties could use
their good offices and bring about good
terms. This is the only hope for a ter
mination of the war which diplomats
can see. >
Solemn services in honor' of those
who fell in the defense of Port Arthur
were held on Sunday in the Kazan ca
thedral and the Troitka monastery.
The service passed off without demon
stration of any sort. Neither the Em
peror nor any member of the imperial
family was present. A requiem was
held simultaneously in the chapel
Tsarskoe-Selo.
COSTLY TO CHINA.
Land of Confucius Mast Pay Part of
Japan's War Bills.
TACOMA, Jan. 8. âSam B. Trissel,
the Associated Press correspondent
who has Just returned from the seat
of war, in an interview to-day said:
When peace coimb In the Orient the Jap
anese wilWifw Korea and China will rayM'tr
Manchuria and pay the freight. I am willing"
to go on record with that prediction. I say
that China will be obliged to bear a large
share of the expense of the war because no
Intelligent man believes that Russia can be
compelled to pay war indemnity. She will lose
territory which she has tried to grab, but her
home resources and her international position
are too great to admit of the possibility of her
being forced to pay any bills but her own. She
will be forced to recede from an unwarranted
rositlon. Nothing more can be extorted from
her.
China will be compelled to share Japan's
war expense, because Japan is fighting the
battles of that nation as well as her own.
When an international convention settles the
terms of peace it undoubtedly will be decided
that China's preservation from dismemberment
Is the boon for which she ought to pay. and
the powers will fix the price. China will have
nothing to sayâshe ran simply pay.
Even the poor in the United States
and England eat white bread. In most
of the continental countries of Europe
rye bread Is the staple. The Russians
use buckwheat. The Laplanders have a
bread made of oaten meal mixed with
pine bark, and the Icelanders make
their flour from lichens. Banana flour
Is used in the South Sea islands.

NO LINE ON CASUALTIES.
War Department Unable to Figure Up
Losses in the Far East.
WASHINGTON, Jan. B.âln despair
the War Department has giving up try
ing to get an accurate line on the total
of casualties in the Russo-Japanese
war. At the outbreak of hostilities in
the Far East a War Department clerk
of German birth and unsophisticated
mind started to make a record from the
press reports of losses. He forgot to
take into consideration the fact that
the Russians call a battle by one name
and the Japanese by another and that
official reports straggle in days after
the first news and are frequently con
fused with later events.
At the end of 1904 the clerk began to
foot up his figures and found that they
showed the Russians had lost 4,397,000
men and the Japanese 5,778,000 men.
His visions of making money out of
the first "authentic record of losses"
took wings right there and he gave up
the job.
The bureau of military information of
the War Department adopted a similar
course at the beginning of the war. but
when the totals showed more men lost
than there are soldiers, non-combatants
and neutrals in all Manchuria they, too,
turned their attention to more profit
able lines.
STANDARD OIL
IN NEW FIELDS
Manager of Big Corporation
Seeking to (let Control of
Gas Companies in South
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. BâHenry H.
Rogers, manager of the Standard Oii
Company, is negotiating for the pur
chase of the various Lowe gas com
panies of Southern California. Leopold
Wallach, who effected the sale of the
San Francisco Coke and Gas Company
(a Lowe concern), has been in Los An
geles conferring with the Lowe people.
He has made a proffer to some of the
heaviest stockholders and this fs now
under consideration.
There are three Lowe companies
operating in Southern California, the
Independent Gas Company, capitalized
at $3,000,000, and proposing to sell gas
at wholesale; the Los Angeles Sub
urban Gas Company, capitalized at
$3,000,000, with about ten miles of
mains, now doing business with retail
customers, and the California Coke
and Gas Company, capitalized at $1,
--000,000, supplying customers in Pasa
dena, South Pasadena and Long Beach.
If the Standard Oil succeeds in its
ambition here, or rather, if the Lowe
people succeed in selling to the Stand
ard Oil, the Los Angeles Gas and Elec
tric Company will have a rival worthy
of its steel. The Los Angeles Company
has hitherto bought or beaten out its
rivals with ease, and the Lowe com
panies, though now operating, have not
proven formidable competitors.
TO RACE AGAINST DEATH
ACROSS THE ATLANTIC
Medlll Pattersons of Chicago Will
Hurry to Bedside of the
Countess Gyzicki.
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âMrs. Robert
Medill Patterson, Joseph Medill Pat
terson, her son, and his wife, formerly
Miss Alice Higginbotham of Chicago,
arrived in this city to-day on a race
against death to Europe. They will
sail on the first steamship this week,
their destination being Vienna, where
Countess Gyzicki, Mrs. Patterson's
daughter, lies critically ill with acute
peritonitis.
Countess Gyzicki was Miss Eleanor
Patterson, daughter of the well-known
Chicago publisher. She was married
last April to Count Gyzicki of Russia
at the home of her mother in Wash
ington. The young couple have since
been living at the country estate of
the Count, near Kieff, Russian Poland,
and It was there that the Countess was
taken seriously ill.
LONDON STOCKS NOT UPSET
BY FALL OF PORT ARTHUR
Feeling of Nervousness About Internal
Situation in Russia Reported
in France.
LONDON, Jan. B.âThe fall of Port
Arthur had comparatively a small ef
fect on the Stock Exchange, Russian
stocks not suffering to any great ex
tent, and the market generally re
maining fairly firm. In France a feel
ing of nervousness about the internal
situation in Russia is beginning to be
observed, which has had the effect of
weakening the markets the last two
days.
The American market waa unsettled
by the prospect of continued Northern
Securities litigation. Prices have been
unsettled and the close was rather
weaker.

BUSINESS MEN
URGE NEW LAWS
Long List of Suggestions
to Be Taken Up by the
National Board of Trade
INTEBEST IN SHIPPING
Various Bodies Desire Some
Legislation to Help Build
Up the Merchant 3larine
WASHINGTON, Jan. B.âThe pro
gramme of recommendations and reso
lutions to be considered at the thirty
fifth annual meeting of the National
Board of Trade, which will begin here
on Tuesday, January 17, has been made
public. An unusually large number of
resolutions of interest to the business
interests of the country have been pro
posed by representative commercial
bodies for consideration, chief among
which are those relatiÂ»g to the mer
chant marine, the interstate commerce
laws, reciprocity, uniform bills of lad
ing, reduction of the tax on alcohol
and improvement of various water
ways.
\ arious boards of trade and trans
portation and chambers of commerce
have presented resolutions urging Con
gressional legislation for the upbuild
ing of the American merchant marine.
In various resolutions enlarged powers
are asked for the Interstate Commerce
Commission. The New York Board of
Trade, by resolution, expressed itself
as opposed to the bill conferring the
rate-making power on the commission.
The board expresses the opinion that
it seems wiser, for the present at least,
to rely on the recently applied methods
of enforcing the decision of the com
mission by injunction rather than to
enact the Cooper-Quarles bill, the pro
visions of which it holds may be con
strued to be much more far-reaching
than even its advocates are willing to
defend or consent to.
Railroad pooling is favored in several
resolutions and so is the institution by
the Government of a parcels post, such
as several foreign nations now enjoy.
The adoption of one-cent postage for
Invoices and the improvement of the
Mlsslsippl River and of ' coastwise
waterways are also urged. The impor
tance of international arbitration
treaties is set forth in a number of
resolutions and an expression is made
for an early ratification of those now
pending. *
The Scranton (Pa.) Board of Trade
requests the adoption of a resolution
asking that the President Join other
powers in a determined effort to com
pel Russia and Japan to submit their
differences to The Hague tribunal for
arbitration.
The Boston Chamber of Commerce
wants reciprocity with Canada, and
other commercial bodies ask that our
foreign trade be extended by treaties
of a reciprocal character. Free alcohol
for industrial purposes is advocated by
the Cincinnati Chamber of Commerce
and a reduction of the tax on that
commodity by the Philadelphia Trades
League.
The Sugar Manufacturers' Associa
tion of America urges that If reduc
tions be made in the existing tariff on
goods or articles imported from the
Philippines, Jthere should be excepted
from such reductions such articles
grown, produced or manufactured in
the United States as would be serious
ly affected by such reductions.

SUFFER FROM SICKNESS

CHICAGO, Jan. B.âWhile various
kinds of epidemics have affected the

ness, Including Overseer Dowle, who,
It is now admitted, undertook the trip

to become epidemic.

MAY END FIGHT
BY COMPROMISE
Factions on Statehood Bill
May Agree to Eliminate
Arizona and New Mexico
POSSIBLE LAST RESORT
Enemies of Measure Do Not
Oppose Admission of the
Other Two . Territories
I WASHINGTON, Jan. B.âThe state
hood bill will continue to be the prin
cipal topic of discussion in the Senate
during the present week, but other
measures will receive attention each
day during the morning hours, includ
ing the omnibus claims bill, for which
Senator Warren stands sponsor. An
effort will be made to get through the
bill providing for the compensation of
American fishermen whose vessels
were seized previous to the arbitration
of 1593.
The pure food bill will remain in the
background for the present, not be
cause the friends of'that measure have
abandoned it, but* because they con
sider that its chances will be improved
by not pressing for immediate consid
eration.
Discussion of the statehood question
will begin on Monday with a speech by
Senator Morgan. He will be followed
by other opposing Senators. The pres
ent plan of the opposition is to keep
the discussion going until some of the
appropriation bills are reported and it
becomes necessary to take them up:
or, if this is postponed too long, to dis
place the bill with the pure food bill.
Failing In all these expedients, they
probably will seek a compromise.
The only real fight Is against the
uniting of Arizona and New Mexico,
and there is talk of eliminating those
Territories entirely from the statehood
proposition. It is believed if this were
done the bill for the consolidation of
Oklahoma and Indian Territory would
pass.
It is the Intention of the House to
begin active consideration of appro
priation bills during the present week.
Monday is District of Columbia day
and the resolution providing for the
ceremonies of the inauguration of
.President Roosevelt and deciding
where the inauguration ball shall be
held will be considered.
PIONEERS PROTEST.
Arizonans Object to the Joint State
hood Bill.
TUCSON. Ariz., Jan. B.âAt a meet
ing of the Society of Arizona Pioneers
this afternoon an earnest protest
against the merger of Arizona and
New Mexico jointly into a State was
adopted. In substance it follows:
Ye declare that the pledge of the Federal
Government In the enactment of the organic
law creating Arizona Into a territorial govern
ment and defining the geographical boundaries,
in which It was agreed Arizona should enjoy
Territorial government until erected Into a
State of the Union, was a sacred compact,
which the pioneer citizens of Arizona (compris
ing the sons and daughters of every State of
the Union) accepted In good faith, and In con
sideration thereof have given years of suffering
and tell, enduring great privations and hard
ships at the risk of life in conquering, develop
ing, civilizing and the building up of the pros
perity of the State of Arizona.
We plead that the compact may be kept In
violate by the Government, and only changed
or modified upon the consent ot the citizens of
Arizona, whose rights should be considered,
and we earnestly pray that, If determined to
pass the joint Statehood bill now before the
Senate, It be with the Foraker amendment,
leaving the question of merger to the vote of
the citizens of the respective Territories.
Gratitude is expressed to Senators
Bard, Foraker, Baker and others for
standing by Arizona.

EX-GOVERNOR DEAD.
Lloyd Lowndes of Maryland Suddenly
Passes Away.
CUMBERLAND, Md., Jan. 8. â
Lloyd Lowndes, ex-Governor of Mary
land, died suddenly at his home here
to-day.
He arose at 8:30 o'clock this morn
ing, took a bath and began to dress
preparatory to attending church serv
ices. His sudden fall attracted the at
tention df Mrs. Lowndes, who was in
the room. She summoned assistance
and the unconscious form was placed
upon the bed. Physicians were has
tily summoned and were quickly at
the bedside, but death had occurred
before their arrival. Death was due
to heart failure.
Lowndes was 60 years of age. He
leaves five sons.

DIVORCE ISSUE
IS DISCUSSED
I David Starr Jordan, Rev. D.
C. Gardner and Attorney
Wright Give Their Views
i TRINITY CHURCH FILLED
â¢
Big Congregation Listens
to Opinions of Lawyer,
Educator and Clergyman
âÂ«
In a noteworthy discussion of the
divorce question, Dr. David Starr Jor
dan, president of Stanford University;
the Rev. D. Charles Gardner, chap
lain of Memorial Chapel, Stanford
University, and John A. Wright, the
attorney, occupied Dr. Clampett's pul
pit at Trinity Church last night. In
spite of the inclemency of the weather,
the stately church was filled.
Dr. Clampett expressed his appre
ciation of the large attendance under
such conditions and spoke of the sub
ject which was to be treated by the
speakers, declaring that it affected the
sanctity and the purity of the homes
of the nation. He said the laxity in
the matter of divorce became a serious
menace to the life of the American
republic and the community, which
should welcome anything that would
draw serious attention to the existing
conditions.
The Rev. Mr. Gardner, speaking
"On the Christian Obligation of Mar
riage." said that In the United States,
and especially in California, the peo
ple were face to face with a serious
looseness of public sentiment on the
subject of marriage and divorce. The
record showed 60,000 divorces in one
year, 1650 being in San Francisco, a
record which he thought boded ill for
the welfare of society. In it he saw
the lack of reverence for the marriage
state and a woeful lack of stability In
public sentiment. *
The purity and sanctity of the mar
riage relation, he held, was so bound
up with human happiness and social
order that it needed to be safeguarded
by a refined and intelligent social sen
timent and based upon sound moral
principles. He had long felt that the
mending and making of canons on di
vorce and remarriage to control the
members of particular churches was
not enough and that the time was ripe
for a campaign of education as to the
i right idea of marriage itself.
AN IDLE MAN OR WOMAN.
President Jordan treated the "So
cial Side" of the subject and pointed
out in his characteristically clear
manner that "It takes leisure to be
unhappy, and that in every divorce
! case there was at least one Idle man
or idle woman."
"Marriage," said Dr. Jordan, "rep
: resents the most important incident in
the life of a man or woman, and di
vorce is the confession of failure.
There are two classes of divorcesâ
the Inevitable and the trivial. It Is
clear that a general divorce law should
exist and that remarriage should be
delayed.
"There are three classes of poorâthe
Lord's poor, the devil's poor and pau
pers. So with divorces there are the
Lord's divorces, the devil's divorces and
the divorces of those who cannot have
partners."
The speaker pointed out the pitiable
situation of young women sold by their
parents, caught by a glitter of one
good trait among bad ones, and mated
to disease and moral death.
"Men," said Dr. Jordan, "place their
wives In squalid boarding-houses, fine
'or shabby, with only make believe dv-
I ties, with nothing to hear but gossip,
! nothing to think but personalities,
nothing to read but sentimental trash
| and no amusement save folly and vul
garity. It Is no wonder then that there
Is deterioration."
DANGER IN STAGNATION.
He disclaimed being an advocate of
the social whirl, but that, he said, was
better than stagnation: Neither did he
advocate a life of money-making, but
he considered that money-grabbing
gave strength of a kind.
"Anything," he was sure, "is better
than a clammy life warmed with red
pepper and whisky." ,
John A. Wright spoke on the present
i aspect of marriage and divorce from a
civil lawyer's point of view, and in a
lucid manner explained the law of mar
riage and the contract created by the
parties to the marriage. He showed
i where States refused to recognize di
vorces granted in other States. In
America there were, he said, fifty dif
ferent jurisdictions. In an expression
of high regard for President Roosevelt
he suggested that there should be an
amendment to the United States con
stitution and that all churches unite
on one day in a petition for a constitu
tional amendment giving Congress
power to establish a uniform law of
marriage and divorce.

LIST OF CANCER
VICTIMS GROWS
Boston Expert Alleges That
City of San Francisco Has
the Largest Percentage
USE OF KNIFE ADVISED
Report of Harvard Commis
sion on Its Investigation
as to Proper Remedies
Special Dispatch to Tht Call.
BOSTON. Jan. B.âDr. Emmens
Briggs of this city, an expert, states
that there has been an alarming in
crease in cancer within the last forty
years and Boston is now the second
city in the country In point of victims
of the scourge. San Francisco having
a larger percentage. According to his
figures, the increase in Boston has been
from 2.8 to each 10,000 population in
1883 to 8.5 for the same number lis 1903.
while the proportion for the whole
world Increased only from 8.85 to 9.95
In 10,000
Harvard experts, however, who have
been investigating cancer for two
y. j ars, pronounce the disease non-In
fectious and non-contagious.
Dr. E. H. Nichols, head of the Har
vard cancer commission, says that it
is a hereditary affliction. The com
mission has prepared a report as to
the remedies, which advises early use
of the knife instead of drugs or
serums.
STEVEDORE FATALLY
INJURED BY A FALL
Drops Twenty Feet Into the Hold of
Collier Moivtara, Lying at
Adams Dock.
OAKLAND, Jan. B;âJohn Marks,
30 years old, a stevedore, fell twenty
feet into the hold of the collier Mon
tara this evening at Adams wharf,
sustaining a fatal fracture of the skull
at the base of the brain.
Marks had just reported for work
on the night shift, wh,ere he had been
helping to unload the steamer's big
coal cargo. He inadvertently stepped
into an open hatch, falling before he
could recover himself. The injured
man was taken to the. Receiving: Hos
pital, where he died at 8:30. His resi
dence was at 652 Madison street.
Marks leaves a wife and two young
children. The body was removed to
the Morgue.

MARE ISLAND'S
FOES ARE BUSY
Talk of Closing the Navy
Yard at Vallejo Is Re
vived in Washington
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âThe Tribune
publishes the following In Its naval
gossip from Washington:
"The Navy Department Is in favor of
closing Mare Island navy yard, or at
least reducing it to a third or fourth
class yard. The greatest difficulty con
tinues to be encountered In the ap
proach to the yard, and It Is found
that the largest cruisers and battle
ships must go to Puget Sound, as the
depth of the water in the channel of
the Mare Island navy yard is not suffi
cient to admit those ships. It is pro
posed to establish a navy yard some
where else In San Francisco Bay. and
several localities are under considera
tion.
"Rear Admiral Endlcott, chief of the
Bureau of Yards and Docks, and other
officers who know something of the
conditions at Mare Island, are in favor
of closing or transferring the yard, al
though they are willing that another
year's trial shall be made of keeping
the channel open."

FINDS PULPIT
DESPITE COURT
Preacher Barred From Regu
lar Church by Injunction
Soon Finds New Quarters
Special Dispatch to The Call.
TACOMA, Jan. B.âBarred from using
his church by a temporary restraining
order issued yesterday. Rev. B. A.
Sand, pastor of Zion Norwegian Luth
eran Church at Everett, preached to
day in Normanna Hall. As far as he
is concerned the church building will
remain empty. Until final action be
taken on the Injunction he cannot use
the building.
Action for a permanent Injunction
was begun several days ago by Princi
pal Dardon of Bethania School. It Is
the result of a church wrangle which
has been brewing for some time*. Some
of the students of Bethania School have
taken the Rev. Mr. Sand's part and
criticize their principal for "mixing In
church politics and bringing lawsuits."
Instead of "attending to his classes."
Sand's friends say the cake probably
will be settled out of court.
DINNER TO PARDEE.
Governor Guest at Repast Furnished
by Senator Lukens.
SACRAMENTO. Jan. B.âGovernor
Pardee was the principal guest at a
duck dinner given at a, local rotlsserie
last, night by Senator Lukens of Ala
meda. The birds were supplied by
Cal Ewing and J. Walker Scott,
who said they shot them. Many State
officials, legislators and other promi
nent people were present.
Lieutenant W. E. Safford, recently of
the navy, but now of the Department
of Agriculture, has about two hundred
b<roks from the library of Robert Louis
Stevenson. Among them is the Bible
which belonged to Stevenson's father.
Lieutenant Safford bought the books in
Samoa and took them to Guam and
Manila with him when he was on duty
In those places.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL. MONDAY, JANUARY 9, 1905.
The golden Jubilee of St. Mary's
Church (Paulist fathers), the Rev. H.
H. Wyman rector, was observed yes
terday. Archbishop Montgomery being
celebrant at solemn pontifical mass at
11 o'clock. He was assisted by the Rev.
Father M. J. Connolly as deacon and
Rev. Father J. E. Cottle as subdeacon.
The Rev. Father Michael Otis was mas
ter of ceremonies.
The Very Rev. Father Prendergast.
Vicar General, delivered the sermon
and said in part:
There wu ample room In this broad land of
California for the aboriginal inhabitants and
for the new race, and It la very probable that
the founders of thlÂ» commonwealth would have
found means of preserving the old missions had
they not been brought to desolation by the
Mexican policy of secularization before the
stars and stripes first waved over the shores of
Monterey.
Unfortunately, by that time many of the In
dians had abandoned the missions; soma of the
Franciscan fathers were. dead, or had with
drawn broken-hearted from the scenes of their
devoted labors; some of the missions were al
ready despoils of strangers; the first Bishop of
California. .Garcia Diego, was sleeping the
peaceful sleep of the just, and Santa Barbara
and its fine mission church were about to yield
precedence to San Francisco and thla the first
St. Mary's Cathedral. It was. however, for
tunate that Archbishop Alemany was appointed
first Archbishop of San Francisco. He was
qualified in every respect to be the connecting
link between the old and the new.
A Spaniard by birth, but a missionary for
years in Ohio and Tennessee, he was in full
sympathy with American ideas and was uni
versally respected by the American people,
while he was held in veneration alike for his
office and his personal virtues by the old race.
. Under him that race gradually disappeared
with honor, and the new entered upon Its great
career.
Father Prendergast dwelt upon the
changes in the environments of the old
cathedral, which made the building of
the new cathedral necessary. He also
referred to -the coming of the Paulist
fathers from New York to preserve the
old church, perpetuate its work and to
restore to it something of Its former
glory.
There were many pastors present,
also representatives from Jesuit,
Salesian and Franciscan orders, and
the Right Rev. Monsignor Ferrant,
vicar and patriarch of Constantinople,
was in the sanctuary.
The decorations, which were very
beautiful, were the work of the Misses
Louise. Clara and Cecilia Daly and
Mrs. Sally Walter.
GOULD PLEADS
FOR STUDENTS
His Letter Aids to Secure
Reinstatement of the Four
Suspended Columbia Boys
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âFollowing: the
receipt of abetter from Klngdon Gould,
asking clemency for the four students
who were suspended in connection with
an attempt to seise him for the sooho
enores' dinner Just before the holidays,
announcement has been made by Dean
Hutton of the school of applied sci
ences of Columbia University that the
disciplined menâAlgeltlnger, Cauchois,
Doty and Finchâwill be allowed to re
turn to their work at the reopening of
the university this week.
Before the announcement was made
there was a conference between Presi
dent Butler, the members of the board
of student representatives and the four
suspended students. The purpose of
disciplining the four men was to make
plain to the students themselves and
to the public the fact that hazing
would not be tolerated at Columbia,
and that end, it is said, has been ac
complished. /
Kingdon Gould in his letter asking
that the suspended men be allowed to
return accounted for his possession of
the revolver, which he fired to frighten
off the sophomores, and expressed his
wish to finish his course on terms of
good fellowship with the other stu
dents of the university. Regarding the
revolver he wrote: /
I always take long ride* in the country after
the college work of the day. usually returning
after dark. I frequently take with me a pis
tol. On the day in question I happened to
have it, and when attacked I fired it in the
i air so that by the consequent surprise of the
fellows I might gain time and opportunity to
reach the frat house, where I was going to
eet my lunch. I had not the slightest inten
tion of doing harm to any one. and never sup
posed the Incident would be taken seriously.

PITIFUL ENDING
OF RUINED LIFE
Bettina Girard, Once the
Belle of Washington, Dies
Raving With Delirium
HER CAREER A TRAGEDY
Beginning Amid Refined
Surroundings, It Sinks
to Gutter and Workhouse
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âDeath ended
the eventful career of Bettina Girard
in Roosevelt Hospital early this morn
ing, where she was taken raving- and
delirious, under the name of Elizabeth
Witter, from a house in East Forty
first street on Saturday.
The career of Bettina Girard was full
of contrasts, over which scientists
puzzled. Beginning amid the refined
surroundings of a wealthy and dis
tinguished family in Washington, it
sank as low as the gutter and the
workhouse, to rise and fall, year in
and year out.
One week upon the stage, showing
flashes of brilliancy and with features
and form little changed, she would ap
pear, only to be found the next week
helpless in the street from vices that
held her absolutely captive.
Bettina Girard was 35 years of age,
a daughter of General Albert Ordway,
the famous soldier. From her earliest
childhood two sentiments appeared to
possess herâone an inordinate love of
admiration and the other a desire f#r
sensation.
Stories are told of the pranks of the
girl at a quiet little convent in George
town and of her departure from there
to finish her education under tutors;
of her daring horsemanship and love
of novelty; finally of her debut in
Washington society. Pretty, vivacious
and brilliant, she immediately became
the leader of the younger set. She was
the toast of army and navy. At a
part"' a young diplomat asked:
"What must I do to become
famous?"
"I will show you," she said,-laugh
ingly.
There was a whisk of drapery and
the young woman stood holding aloft
her slipper. Filling It with champagne,
she held it toward the young diplo
mat.
"Drink a toast to fame," she com
manded, laughingly.
Nothing daunted, the toast was
drunk. *
After being divorced from wealthy
Arthur Padelford, she became an
actress. It was then that her mental,
moral and physical eccentricities be
gan to develop. She at once entered
the whirl of gayety. No constitution,
however rugged, could stand the strain.
Drugs, to which she had recourse,
drink and kindred dissipation soon as
serted themselves and it was not long
before police courts, sanitariums and
hospitals had her name daily on their
records.
She was married seven times. One
husband was a millionaire and six were
actor? or vaudeville artists.
MAKES ESCAPE
FROM ASYLUM
Former Unitarian Pastor De
clares That He Was Sane
When He Was Committed
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MONTREAL, Quebec, Jan. B.âlncar
ceration in an asylum by persons who
are said to have first bereft him of his
church in Gouverneur, N. V., and an
escape by night to Canada, form an
epitome of alleged persecution told by
Rev. Ure Gordon Mitchell, a British
subject, and for some time pastor of
the Unitarian church at Gouverneur.
Mitchell said that in 1898 he was sent
to Gouverneur by the New York State
body of the church. He labored there
until a short time ago, when, he said,
false charges were made against him,
but he fought them down. He con
tinued:
Then, for the last six years of my residence
in Gouverneur, my wife, with whom 1 had
been living in peace for about fifteen years,
became imbued with the doctrines of the (Chris
tian Scientists, whose theories were repugnant
to me. She plagued me incessantly with books
and pamphlets, and every 'lay harangued me
until my life was a burden. After a particu
larly aggravating session In August of 1904 I
snatched up the books, and, placing them in
a stove, burned them.
Three days later I was walking down one of
the principal itrcets of the town when I was
called into the effice of one of my opponents.
"He would like to see you," the office boy told
me. I entered the inside office and found my*
self In a trap. My opponent was sitting there,
and as I entered a couple of deputy sheriffs
entered by the back door.
"You have been acting strangely," he said.
"We are here to examine you as to your sani
ty."
I was thunderstruck. A couple of his fol
lowers went through the farce of an examina
tion and without further ado I was committed
by a magistrate to the Point Erie Asylum,
near Ogdensburg.
There 1 spent three harrowing months, and If
sane when I entered I was constantly afraid
that my sanity would give way under the hor
rors of that confinement. 1 was tortured by
the attendants, but finally made my escape by
night and fled to Canada. I will ask the Brit
ish Government to take up my case.
Honored by Millions.
The total admissions to the World's Fair
were 19,000,000; of this number approximately
3,000,000 were officers, exhibitors, concession
aires and employes; 6,000,000 were residents
of St. Louis and 10,000,000 non-residents. Es
timating that each non-resident visited the fajr
at least four times, it would appear that 2.
--800,000 people came to St. Louis in the seven
months and during this same period more than
1.260.000 people passed through the Anheuser-
Busch plant. Never before has an establish
ment been honored by so many visitors, and it
is safe to say that at least one-half the people
who came to St. Louis availed themselves of
the opportunity to inspect the greatest plant of
Its kind in the world.
1904 was the banner year in the history of
the Anheuser-Busch Brewing Association, its
sales being 1.265,711 barrels of beer, showing
an Increase of 163,949 barrels. The latter as
an annual output would exceed that of the
majority of the larger breweries of this coun
try. This phenomenal increase is the more re
markable because the past year, owing to the
long winter and abnormally cool summer, was
a decidedly off year for the beer business. Ths
total gain of the beer output for the United
States for 1904 was about 900.000 barrels, thus
proving Anheuser-Busch's Increase of 163,949
barrels to be about 30 per cent of that of all
breweries combined. Such an exceptionally
good showing can only be attributed to the ex
cellence of their products and the courteous
treatment of their patrons.
A number of companies are prospect
ing for petroleum In various parts of
Mexico. A gusher has recently been
struck near Tampico. on the property
of the Mexican Petroleum Company,
which has been sinking wells in the
region for the last two or three years.
Other wells are being sunk near the
gusher, and experts believe that the
company is about to be rewarded for
the money it has spent upon prospect
ing by the finding of oil in paying
quantities.

Flint's Supporters Manifest Greatest Elation/
SENATOR BAUER TAKES ADVICE PRE
SUMABLY FROM JESSE MARKS.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Jan. B.âAll the can
didates for United States Senator who
were here last week have returned to
the scene of action with renewed con
fidence of ultimate success. The Flint
forces outwardly manifest the greatest
elation and F>lnt himself Is in a happy
frame of mind. It is known to the

Angeles candidate on the first ballot,
and may decide to stay in that camp

The Bard forces are elated also, but

BARD'S MEN ARE FIRM.
E. O. Gerberding. Frank J. Bran
don, C. T. Elliott, Thomas Hughes. C.
A. Young, David Perkins, James A.
Louttit and Dr. Rowell were at Sena
tor Bard's headquarters to-night. They

received. The notion that there Is a
wavering or weakness In the 3ard
column seems to be absurd. The
alignment for Bard will be hard to
break. It Is said by the politicians to
be the firmest bunch In the field. The
Republicans who have avowed their
intention of supporting Bard on the
first ballot will go' the whole route
with him.
The Republicans of the Bard camp
are stalwarts. They have not "win
tered or summered" with the old chief
of the Southern Pacific machine and
do not go to Herrin's office for instruc
tions as to their votes for United States
Senator. Bard's strength on the first
ballot may cause more than a ripple
c,f surprise, although he cannot come
here to personally refute the cam
paign canard that he Is 80 years old
and very feeble.

PERSIA PAYS
FOR A MURDER
Makes Partial: Reparation
for Death of Rev. Labarree,
an American Missionary
WASHINGTON, Jan. B.âlnformation
has reached the State Department
that, complying with the insistent de
mands of the United States, the Per
sian Government has made to the
widow partial reparation for the mur
der of the Rev. Benjamin W. Labar
ree, an American missionary, by a
gang of fanatics and has promised that
all the guilty persons involved in the
crime will be punished. The following
statement regarding the case was
made b.v an official of the State De
partment :
On March 15 last the Department of State
received telegraphic intelligence of the mnrder
of Rev. Benjamin W. Labarree, an American
missionary, near Ouroumla. in Persia, by a
gang of fanatical Kurds.
A demand was immediately made for the
arrest and trial of the murderers, whose
leader, Seyd Mirghafar. was looked i.pon as a
lineal descendant of the Prophet. This cir
cumstance and the fear of arousing religious
disturbances obviously interfered with the
prompt and efficient action on the part of the
local authorities. The most pressing and ear
nest representations of the Ameriaan legation
at Teheran remained fruitless until the 12th
of October, when Mr, Hay instructed the
Minister by cable to make known to the Gov
ernment of the Shah the President's concern
in the adequate punishment of all the crim
inals and hts Intention to lay the matNr be
fore Congress, with appropriate recommebda
tl' ns if his demands for full justice were fur
ther delayed.
The murderers were thereupon arrested, but
the Persian Government, holding the life-of a
descendant of the Prophet sacred, offered a
pecuniary Indemnity in lieu of the death pen
alty of Mirghafar, and promised execution
of his accomplices. After consultation with the
widow of the Rev. Mr. the offer was
accepted and an indemnity of $3".<w> âgreatly
in excess of the cum named by the widow â
was paid to the American legation on June 3.
Solemn assurance was given that the guilty
would receive effective and sufficient punish
ment and that no special tax would be levied
on Christians In the province to recover the
amount of the Indemnity.
The President has complimented United
States Minister Pearson at Teheran for the
energetic and efficient manner In which he
has managed the case there.
Causes of Poverty.
That a certain number of the pov/
erty strickenâhow large a proportion
no one knows âare the result of vicious
ness in our social system there seems
little doubt. If the viciousness could
be located and corrected these paupers
would become self-supporting and self
respecting citizens. The thriftless,
able-bodied ne'er-do-wells, whom
neither education nor opportunity,,can
improve, could at least be forced to
earn their own living and cease to be
a burden on the workers of the world.
With these two classes of mendicants
eliminated, there would still remain
enough of the flotsam and jetsam of
humanity, helpless through mental or
physical weakness or disease, to fully
occupy the charitable Instincts of the
race.
It may" be considered doubtful
whether the science of sociology has
.reached a point where a poverty com
mission, relatively similar in purpose to
the monetary t>r sanitary commission,
could throw any light on the subject;
indeed, there are probably as yet few
men competent to serve In such a body.
But it Is a safe prediction that if the
submerged tenth Is ever to be perma
nently raised it will be through the
study of poverty on scientific rather
than sentimental lines.âNew York
Globe.

GRANT' NOT IN FIGHT.
"I am not a candidate for United
States Senator," said Grjant, "and had
not even heard until I got here that
my name had been mentioned. No, I
am not espousing any one's candi
dacy."
"In case it was shown that none of
those now In the race could not be
elected and your friends should see a
good chance for you to win out, would
you get into the race?" was asked.
"No, I will not be a candidate under
any circumstances," replied Grant.

British Steamship Zambesi
Reaches New York After
Series*of Mishaps at Sea
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âThe British
steamship Zambesi, from Savannah for
Bremen with a cargo of cotton and
phosphate, put into this poA this morn
ing after a most eventful voyage, with
her cargo of cotton on Are and with
several of her crew sick. Captain
Rooney of the Zambesi reports that he
sailed from Savannah on New Year's
day. The vessel experienced very se
vere weather. All went well, however,
until January 5, when the vessel struck
a, submerged wreck, which broke the
stem and stove in several of the bow
plates under water, causing the fore
peak to fill with water. The ship was
immediately headed for port, nearly 500
miles distant.
Heavy weather prevailed and furious
seas flooded the forward decks. On
January 6 volumes of smoke were seen
pouring from the fore hatches, and it
was evident the cotton in the hold was
on Are. The ship's hose was quickly
connected and the fire was soon under
control. On Saturday morning five fire
men who were asleep in the forecastle
were discovered in an unconscious con
dition from the fumes of the burning
cotton and phosphate. They were re
moved from the forecastle to the
steamship's cabin, where they revived.
The steamship will discharge the car
go In her forward holds and will prob
ably go into dock for repairs. She hails
from London and is consigned to
Punch, Edye & Co.

"The Parts Herald" states of the Pope Manufacturing Co.'s exhibit at the Paris
Automobile Show the following:
There can be no doubt whatever about the fact that the Pope-Toledo Automo
biles, brought over from America at enormous expense to illustrate to the French
manufacturers the progress that has been made in automobile construction outside

largely on American custom for the disposal of their outputs have been made to see

Measure to Provide
for the Study of
Wheat.
SACRAMENTO. Jan. B.âThe Im
provement of California wheat Is a
subject that Is to receive the attention
of the present Legislature. A bill cov
ering the subject has been prepared by
the Sacramento Valley Development
Association. It will be Introduced In
the Senate by Dlggs and In the Assem
bly by Drew-. It provides for what
shall be known as the California Cereal
Commission, to consist of three mem
bers, and calls for an appropriation of
810,000 to carry on experiments and
tests to develop grains especially suited
to California soil under the direction
of the agricultural experiment station
of the University of California.
Actual work, maintained by private
subscription, is already being carried
on at two stations located in the Sac
ramento and San Joaquin valleys, and
it is desired that the State perpetuate
this work. The bill has the approval
of the California Cereal Improvement
Commission, organized In San Fran
cisco by the various bodies. Including
the Merchants' Exchange of that city*
the State Board of Trade and the Sac
ramento Development Association.
The bill provides that the commis
sioners shall receive mileage, but no
salaries.

THE "THUNDERER" STILL
HOLDS FIRST PLACE
London Times, as for a Century Past,
Yet the World's Greatest
Newspaper.
The Times is still easily the most
authoritative Journal not only in Eng
land, but in the world, and it gives the
cue to all the provincial organs that
share its views. The Times, indeed, is
not a newspaper; it is the Times, it is
an institution, a necessity, as much a
part of the unchallenged order of
things as the monarchy itself. A short
time ago when the Times was reducing
the price of its subscription to $15 a
year, the editor of a great London
daily remarked to me that the move
was a mistake. "Instead of lowering
the price," he said, "they should double
it. It now stands at threepence a
copy (6 cents); they should make. It
sixpence a copy (12 cents). I cannot
imagine them losing a single subscrib
er. Everybody who is anybody In
England has to have the Times, what
ever its cost"
Â«I do not know whether my friend's
calculation was economically sound,
but it shows what is thought of the
Times that one of the shrewdest edi
tors in London could launch the Na
poleonic idea of publishing it at 12
cents a day. That the Times Is some
times heavy, that it is often preju
diced and intolerant, especially on
Irish affairs, is undeniable; but its un
rivaled foreign service, the excellence
and authority of its special articles
from all countries and on all political
subjects, and its magnificence in
throwing open its correspondence col
umns to arguments on all sides of
every public question more than Jus
tify Its reputation and more than ex
plain Its position and Influence. It
comes nearer to being a national forum
than any journal in the world.âHar
per's Weekly.

FACTS ABOUT WHEAT

WILL ISSUE BULLETINS ON THEIR WORK
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,

Professor E. J. Wickson of the agri
cultural department of the State Uni
versity returned last night from a tour

the trip being preliminary to an ex
haustive examination of the wheat
grown in every part of California, to
determine what localities produce
wheat richest in gluten and v.hat con
ditions are responsible for the wheat
found to contain most gluten.
Professor Wickson's work is being

other commercial bodies of California.

BAR SOCIETY
TO REAWAKEN
Attorneys Arrange at a Pre
liminary Banquet to Put
. New Life Into Organization
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 8.
Steps are to be taken to reorganize
the Alameda County Bar Association.
The plans provide for increasing the
membership of the society and then
making it an influential factor in the
community, as well as a helpful aid to
its individual members.
Under the new regime meetings will
be held more frequently than they have
been, and those who make up its
strength will be brought closer together
socially and In a business way. A meet
ing will be held in April to choose of
ficers for the year. This will probably
be followed by a banquet, to be held at
the Claremont Country Club.
Only one meeting of the organization
has been held since the death of its
'president, Judge J. H. Smith, a year
. ago. This was a gathering Friday
night around the banquet board, where
M. C. Chapman was toastmaster. Much
'of the local legal talent was heard In
addresses outlining the plans that are
shortly to be carried out.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 9, 1905.
DOES NOT CARE
FOR THE COIN
Berkeley Ofliceseeker In
sists That Salary of De
sired Job Be Not Raised
SPRINGS NOVEL POINT
Town Trustee Opposes Plan
to Increase Emoluments
Even of Place He Wants
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street, Jan. 8.
Redmond C. Staats, member of the
Board of Town Trustees, has thrown
a bombshell into the ranks of the poli
ticians in Berkeley by announcing his
candidacy for the office of City At
torney 'and in the same breath de
claring that he will strenuously op
pose any proposal to raise the salary
of the office, as is contemplated by a
part of the Board of Trustees.
Staats is believed to be unique in
thus taking the stand of a man who
desires a public office, but who Insists
that he will sturdily fight any plan
which has for its object' the elevation
of the salary of the office in ques
tion.
For some time the Trustees have
had in contemplation the readjust
ment of the salaries of all officials of
Berkeley. It is proposed to increase
the amounts paid to the Trustees, the.
Marshal and his deputies, the Treas
urer, Clerk, Auditor, Recorder, Attor
ney, Tax Collector and the assistants
employed by each.
The ground' for the proposed plan
was found in the progress the city has
made, such progress having carried
Berkeley, it is declared, from the
status of a city of the fifth class to
that of a city of the third class. Those
who wish to see official salaries ele
vated declare that the duties of of
ficials have increased with the growth
of Berkeley and that salaries should
be raised in proportion.
Trustee Staats alone opposes the
proposed horizontal elevation. His
declaration last night of intention to
seek the office of Town Attorney, now
held by Brewton Hayne, and to insist
as a condition of his candidacy that
the salary be kept at its present fig
ure is expected to disarrange the plans
of Staats' confreres. Staats' move is
looked upon as a proceeding likely to
find great favor with the public and
incidentally to advance his interests.
The regular meeting of the Trustees
is to be held to-night and it Is cx
i pected that action will be taken upon
| the proposal to revise the official sal
ary roll of the university town.
POWER PLANT
UNDERGOES TEST
Big Boilers in Building on
University Campus Ready
for Work They Are to Do
Berkeley Office San Francisco Call,
2148 Center Street. Jan. 8.
Tests have been made by the con
tractors in charge of the construction
of the university's expensive and elab
orate heating and lighting plant, pre
liminary to the connecting of the ma
chinery with various university build
ings to which the plant will supply
heat, light and power. The connections
will be made next week.
Four buildings on the campus are
ready to be supplied with heat from
the new plant, these being the new
California Hall, Harmon gymnasium,
the Rudolph Spreckels laboratory and
the university library. Live steam will
be carried from the power plant to
these buildings and then hot water
will be sent through the pipes in the
buildings, this being known as the
Plenum system.
According to the figures of Victor H.
Henderson, acting secretary for the
regents, the new power plant will cost
about $60,000. It is paid for out of the
general improvement fund of the uni
versity, which fund receives a fourth
of the two-cent tax on each $100 worth
of property in the State. The brick
work cost $11,136; the three safety
water tube boilers, $9050 10; the oil
burning plant, $9151; a Mackintosh &
Seymour engine, $11,585 60; concrete
foundations, $6990; steam piping and
steam distributing system, $9300.
All the buildings on the campus, and
the grounds as well, are to be l'ghted
with electricity from this plant All
the power needed in each building,
where much power Is constantly re
quired, will be supplied by the new
plant. At present an inadequate power
plant supplies part of the light and
power and some is purchased from
outside companies.
In addition to the advantages gained
from centralizing all the sources of
supply, and adding to its volume, the
authorities say that the danger of fire
in the buildings which will be heated
from the new plant will be greatly
lessened.
PSEUDO CATTLE KING
PASSES BAD CHECK
OAKLAND, Jan. 8. âClaiming to be
possessed of a lordly domain in Okla
homa over which roamed magnificent
herds of grazing cattle, a man giving
his name as Fred Cox managed to get
into debt to J. B. Charpie to the
amount of $95 for board and liquors
and to Robert Knowles the sum of
$70, money borrowed.
Charpie keens a hotel on Stanford
avenue, in Golden Gate, and Knowles
resides in that suburb. In part pay
ment of his account with Charpie,
Cox gave him what purported to be a
check on a bank situated in the coun
try where he said his possessions lay.
It came back indorsed "no funds."
Charpie and Knowles met Cox in the
hotel barroom and demanded their
money. Cox became indignant, drew
a revolver and threatened to shoot his
creditors, but was disarmed before he
could do any damage. Thea he was
sent to the City Prison In the patrol
wagon and locked up and is being held
pending further investigation.

Accosts and Assails Harry C.

Along on Eleventh Street
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,

Harry C. Summers of 632 Telegraph
avenue was knocked out at an early
hour this morning by a blow on the
jaw from a sandbag given him by a
footparj and robbed. The thief has
not been captured.
Summers reported to the police tljat
he was walking along Eleventh street
and when between Webster and Har
rison streets was overtaken by a man,
who walked beside him a few steps,

MANY DEATHS
IN ONE HOUSE
Four Die Within Two Weeks
in a Mission Home and
Superstition Is Roused
Is there something uncanny about
the former home of Frank Schmidt at
347 San Carlos avenue? That is the
question that superstitious people in
the 'Mission are asking. Four of its
tenants died within a few days of one
another and only one survives. Dwell
ers in the neighborhood shudder as
they pass it and speak of it in whis
pers.
Within two weeks four people who
lived at this house of mystery, 347 San
Carlos avenue, sickened and died.
Three passed away in the house and
one died at the City and County Hos
pital. Following is the death record of
the place over which some evil spell
seems to have fallen:
Frank Schmidt, died December 24, of
pneumonia.
John Richardson, his brother-in-law,
died January I, of the same disease.
Mrs. Emma *Richardson. John Rich
ardson's mother, died January 4, of
heart failure.
George Wiser, a young man who at
tended Schmidt and Richardson, was
taken ill of pneumonia and died at the
City and County Hospital January 7.
The startling mortality in the one
house gave rise to the belief that some
contagious disease raged in the neigh
borhood. But an autopsy held on the
remains of young Wiser at the City
and County Hospital revealed conclu
sively that he died from pneumonia.
There is no suspicion that the four
deaths were not all from natural
causes, as all were thoroughly investi
gated. The tragic circumstances can
only be attributed to a strange freak
of the Death Angel.
The only tenant of the 111-fated
house who has survived the visitations
of death is Mrs. Mattie Schmidt,
widow of the first to die, sister of the
second and daughter of the third. Mrs.
Schmidt is heartbroken over her whole
sale bereavement.
WELL-KNOWN PRIEST IS
RUN DOWN BY A CAR
Father M. Stack of Nevada City Badly
Cut and Bruised While Crossing
Sutter Street.
Rev. Father Stack of Nevada City
met with a painful accident last even
ing shortly before 7 o'clock. While
crossing Sutter street at Its junction
with Montgomery he was knocked
down by a cable car and before the
gripman could release his grip from
the cable and put on his brake the
unfortunate clergyman was buffeted
along by the dummy and badly cut
and bruised. Finally succeeding in
stopping his car. the gripman went to
the rescue of the injured man and, at
the latter's request, had him conveyed
to his apartments at the Lick House.
Dr. Whitman, who was summoned
to attend Father Stack, found that his
patient was not injured internally. The
latter's head, however, was badly cut,
as was also his forehead. His face
was severely bruised. Later in the
evening Special Policeman F. J. Hop
per brought Daniel Lynch, the grip
man of the car, to the Hall of Justice,
where he was detained pending an ex
amination into Father Stack's inju
ries.
