RECALLS WORK
OF FIFTY YEARS
Rev. W. A. Brewer Gives
Historical Sermon at St.
John's Church Jubilee
EULOGIZES DR. AKERLY
Communion Serv ice Is Held
in Memory of Former Rec
tor of the Pioneer Parish
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 8.
Fifty ago to-day St John's
Episcopal Church of Oakland was or
ganized, the'opening service being con
ducted by the Rev. E. W. Syle in a
small room over a store on Broadway,
near Fourth street. To-day the mem
bers of the "mother church" of Ala
meda County gathered to celebrate the
golden jubilee of the establishment of
the first Episcopal house of worship on
the eastern shore of San Francisco
Bay.
At an early hour this morning cor
porate communion was celebrated, and
many former members of St. John's
who have moved away from the parish
attended the service. Particularly was
this service a memorial to the Rev.
Benjamin Akerlyâ"Father" Akerly, as
he was lovingly called by the older
membersâw % ho for forty years was rec
tor of St. John's. The communion ser
vice was conducted by the Rev. Edward
F. Gee, rector of St. John's Church, as
sisted by the Rev. H. B. Monges. At
the close of the communion service the
Rev. Charles T. Walkley, rector of St.
Paul's Church, addressed the congre
gation, giving a testimonial of appre
ciation of the great work of Dr. Akerly.
The principal service of the Jubilee
celebration was held at 11 a. m., when
Tours' communion office was sung by a
full chorus choir. Communion was cel
ebrated by the Rev. E. F. Gee, assisted
by the Rev. S. J. Lee as epistoler and
the Rev. David McClure as gospeler.
Dr. McClure preached the dedicatory
sermon at the opening of the present
church, which was erected after the
destruction of the old church by fire in
1896. On this occasion the sermon was
a memorial to Father Akerly. At the
close of the communion service to-day
the Rev. William A. Brewer, registrar
of the diocese, preached the jubilee ser
mon.
RECALLS EARLY DAYS. .
Taking as his text the prayer of Solo
mon at the dedication of the temple.
Dr. Brewer spoke briefly of this the
supreme moment In the life of the
mightiest of Israel's Kings, after which
he said:
It is in just such spirit, I am sure, that
this congregation is gathered to-day. just fifty
years after the founding of St. John's parish,
to recall the memories of the days of old. We
bring to mind the early days of the parish, of
this diocese and of this State. And in the
history of our church on the Paclflo Coast, St.
John's parish Is indeed a pioneer. Of all the
California parishes only three precede Itâ
Trinlu- of .San Francisco, organized on July
22. 1X49; Qraee of San FranciÃco, organized
on April 28, 1850, and St. John's of Stockton,
organized on August 25, 1850. Fourth on this
roll of honor comes St. John's of Oakland, or
ganized January 8, 1855.
And now comes the natural question, "Who
read the church service for the first time un
der due and formal church authority in Oak
land?" When I tell you that It was Bishop
KiD our thoughts will recall with deep affec
tion the stately form and noble features of the
first Bishop of California. In his Journal
Bishop Kip says: "On Wednesday, Novem
ber 15. 1864, I went to Oakland for the pur
pose of making arrangements for the estab
lishment of a church. Owing to some mis
take, notice had not been given for service
on that evening, and I could only take meas
ures by personal conference with the members
of the church residing in that place."
Later on we read In the Journal: "On De
cember 17 I again visited Oakland, where a
commodious room had been arranged for di
vine service. My object was to hold the first
service in this place, which I did. reading;
prayers and preaching morning and after
noon."
Immediately after Bishop Kip's first service
the Rev. FX W. Syle began regular services
In Oakland, his first service being held on
Sunday morning, January 8, 1855. over Beele's
store on Broadway, between Third and Fourth
streets, with a congregation of fourteen per
sons. On the second Sunday in the following
month the first communion was celebrated
with eleven communicants.
The first vestrymen of St. John's Church
were E>. A. Sewerkrop, senior warden; Dr.
Matthew Carter, Junior warden snd clerk; Dr.
Robert Worthlnsrton, Captain John Schand*>r
and Andrew Williams, the first Mayor of Oak
land.
Dr. Benjamin Akerly. who for forty years
was rector of this parish, assumed charge of
St. John's Church on March 1, 1858, after the
return of Mr. Syle to China, where he was a
missionary in Hongkong.
St. John's Church, "Old St. John's" that all
Oaklanders knew so well, was built in 1858.
and on March 1, 1Â«10, the building was con
secrated by Bishop Kip.
THE ALMA MATER.
After teljing of the growth of the
church and of Dr. Akerly's heroic ser
vice in estaolishing other churches.
Dr. Brewer told of the destruction of
the old church by Are, and of the erec
tion and consecration of the present
building. In closing his address he
said:
Now at this fiftieth milestone we behold
this parish, the alma mater, the cherishing:
mother, ever ministering- to her children.
These doors are ever open and day by day
the holy mysteries of our faith are celebrated
at this altar. These fifty years will ever be a
source of Inspiration to your faithful rector,
and it Is our earnest prayer that the story of
the years to come may add new luster to the
precious records of the past.
The Jubilee celebration closed to
night with choral service and an ad
dress by the Right Rev. Sidney C. Par
tridge, Bishop of Kioto, Japan.

YEAR BREAKS
ALL RECORDS
More New Buildings Erected
in Oakland in 1904. Than
in Any Previous Year
NEWCOMERS ARE BUYING
Large Real Estate Deal Made
and Improvements Under
Way at Point Richmond
Oakland Office San Franclsce Call,
1016 Broadway, Jan. 8.
Nearly 1000 new buildings, aggre
gating in value more than $4,000,000,
have been erected In Oakland during
1804. Of these, real estate dealers say
that fully one-third have been built
by newcomers to the city. More than
$1,000,000 has been Invested in new
buildings at the University of Califor
nia, and this, with the other opera
tions throughout the county, will
bring the total cost of new buildings
erected In Alameda County during
1904 close to $6,000,000.
This record eclipses that of any year
in the history of the city by many
thousands of dollars, and the present
outlook indicates that the opening
year will be fully as good as that just
closed, if not better.
Councilman W. J. Baccus, who is
president of the local Bricklayers"
Union, is engaged in the preparation
Of tiie new building ordinance, in
this work he has carefully estimated
the amount of building work done in
the last year. He estimates the cost
of new structures at $4,000,000. Other
men who have been interested in the
subject place the amount at nearly*
$5,000,000.
SALES TO NEWCOMERS.
Local real estate dealers report
that the market has not yet resumed
its wonted activity, but as a quiet sea
son was anticipated after the holidays,
this has not aroused any surprise. The
principal business done during the
opening week has been in small resi
dence properties, a majority of the
sales of the week having been made
to recent arrivals from the Bast and
from other parts of the State. Sev
eral real estate men report that since
the beginning of the year they have
received many inquiries as to the
prices of property in Oakland, and
dealers generally are satisfied that
1905 will be the banner yeaj* for this
city.
A. J. Snyder recently removed his
office from Ninth street to the build
ing formerly occupied by the Contra
Costa Water Company. Mr. Snyder
Is well satisfied with the present con
dition of the market and expects a
busy year. He says:
Indications during the last month, culminat
ing in this week's inquiries, show that the de
mand for Investment properties is on the In
close. In advance It Is safe to predict that
there will be a larger percentage of increase
in Oakland's population during the next year
than during the last. This population must
have homes and as a result even the most
conservative will be compelled to admit that
Oakland- has outdone any previous year.
George H. Lee, who has been asso
ciated with Henry Z. Jones buying
tracts of land and laying them out into
suburban settlements, has just con
cluded the purchase of eighty-three
acres close to the new boulevard, on
the south line and fronting on the east
line of the High street driveway 2000
feet. It is now being put in shape for
sale. It is within the 5-cent fare of two
electric car lines. Those knowing the
section and its present advantages are
congratulating Mr. Lee in securing
such a choice piece of land.
The West Berkeley Improvement Club
has elected the following officers: Pres
ident, Nels Olsen; vice president,
George Grimehaw; secretary, Fred
Hearst; sergeant-at-arms, F. Lane.
AT POINT RICHMOND.
POINT RICHMOND, Jan. B.âOne of
the most Important real estate deals of
the new year was the purchase of
twelve acres of land, a part of the Lu
cas ranch, which is situated on the
main line of the Santa Fe Railroad. The
purchasers of the tract are J. B. Har
ris and D. L. Isgrigg of Berkeley, and
the price paid was $12,000. It is under
stood that the tract will at once be sub
divided into residence lots and placed
on the market.
George C. Henley has Just purchased
the real estate and Insurance business
of H. A. Johnson. Mr. Henley now has
the largest real estate business in Point
Richmond. His offices are located on
Macdonald avenue.
The grading on the line of the East
Shore and Suburban Railroad is now
completed and the work of laying the
track is progressing rapidly. It is ex
pected that the road will be In opera
tion by the middle of February. The
road will connect with the lines of the
Oakland Traction Company. ,
MILLIONAIRE MURRAY
DYING AT ST. FRANCIS
Hemorrhage of the Brain and Par
alysis Brings Montana Banker
Within the Shadow of Death.
Death is hovering about the bedside
of James H. Murray, a millionaire
mining man and banker of Montana,
who has been a guest at the St. Fran
cis Hotel for the last week. On
Wednesday the unfortunate man had
a hemorrhage of the brain and par
alysis followed. Since then he has
been sinking rapidly and last evening
the attending physicians, Drs. Rich
ter, Rosenthal and Nfewmark, enter
tained little hope for their patient's
recovery.
Mr. Murray has not been in good
health for a number of years, and
this fact has caused him and his wife
to spend a great deal of their time
in San Francisco, where they found
the climate more congenial to his con
dition than in Montana. About eight
months ago he purchased the hand
some home of the late Hugh Tevis at
Monterey, where he and his wife have
since been spending much of their
time. The couple came up from Mon
terey two days before he was stricken
down. Murray is one of the best
known , men In Montana, where he
made his immense fortune.

SECRETARY OF HIBERNIA
BANK ILL AT HIS HOME
Sickness of Robert J. Tobln Causes
Concern Among His Friends and
Employes.
Robert J. Tobln, secretary of the
Hibernia Savings and Loan Society,
has been dangerously 111 at his home,
1380 Geary street, for several days.
His condition last, night was somewhat
improved, but he is not yet out of
danger. Though the news of his ill
ness was not noised abroad, Mr. Tobln
has been missed from his office at the
Hibernia Bank and his friends knew
that nothing but illness would keep
him away.
Robert J. Tobln has filled the office
of secretary of the Hibernia Savings
and Loan Society since its creation in
1878. Though the well-known banker
is well along In years, his friends are
confident that he will recover. He
was near to death several years ago,
but recovered after hope had been
abandoned. He Is being attended by
Dr. C. W. Montgomery.

IS TOO SHORT
Thomas F. Egan Declines to
Accept a Place in the
Board of Pnblic Works
FULL TIME OR NOTHING
Appointee Says He Expected
to Serve for Three Years,
but Mayor Proffered One
Thomas F. Egan, one of the recent
appointees of Mayor Schmitz, has de
clined the office of Commissioner of
Public Works. When the offer was
made to him he understood that the
term of office would be for the full
time, three years, and on that condi
tion he was willing to act as one of the
Commissioners. Yesterday morning,
however, when he learned that he was
appointed to act for only one year he
threw up the job and promptly inti
mated to the Mayor that it was to be
for three years or nothing. The Mayor
was taken somewhat aghast when he
learned that Egan would not take the
job.
It is understood the Mayor hopes to
adjust the matter in some way to in
duce Egan to rescind his action. Egan,
in speaking of the matter, said:
I have declined to aocept the office as one of
the Commissioners of Public "Works offered to
me by Mayor Schmitz. It had been my inten
tion to accept tho position until this momins.
when I learned that, instead of the office run
ning for the full term, I was to be appointed
for only one year. This was Indicated In
my formal noUoe of appointment, which I re
ceived this morning. I at once telephoned to
Mayor Schmitz that I could not accept the po
sition on these terms.
â¢ When the matter had been discussed previ
ously between the Mayor and myself I had al
ways supposed the appointment wet Id be for
three years, to fill the place of Miohael Casey.
Upon these conditions, as chairman of the La
bor Union County Committee. I was willing to
take the position. It was never intimated to
me that the office would be other than for the
'full term.
I declined the offer of Mayor Schmlta In no
spirit of vlndietlvecess whatever. When I tele
.phoned to the Mayor tills morning and gave
him my declination he had very little to say
in reply. I believe certain exigencies had
arisen which made a change in his programme.
I do not know what they were, but I think I
have an idea. I have no feeling ot any ani
mosity whatever against the Mayor.
In speaking of the unexpected ac
tion of Egan in throwing up the billet
offered to him, Mayor Schmitz last
night said:
â¢I have received no formal communication
from Mr. Egan in regard to his declination of
.the office I offered to him. It is true that
'there seems to have been some misunderstand
ing between Mr. Egan and myself as to the
t<>rm of the office. He expected, evidently,
thai his appointment would be for three years.
Ithope the matter can be satisfactorily adjust
er!. Cntil I receive a formal notice from him
that he will not act I can do nothing.
SHOWERS KEEP PLEASURE
SEEKERS WITHIN DOORS
Kain Drenches the Garments of Those
Who Refused to Take Weather
Prophet's Tip.
A celestial shower bath opened un
heralded upon the city yesterday,
catching many pleasure-seekers In
their Sunday-go-to-meetings and
drenching the glad rags.
The greater portion of the coast felt
â¢ the effects of the downpour. The rain
started just as the crowds were mak
ing their weekly pilgrimage to the
j.ark music stand. Professor McAdie,
who keeps a census of clouds and
winds, gave out that showers would
probably fall before night. The wise
ones who preferred to "dope" the
cloudlets themselves suffered in con
sequence.
Other than a few mud splashes on
dainty lingerie and chiffons no dam
age was done by the rain in this city.

TO TALK WOOL
AND LIVESTOCK
Joint National Convention
of the Two Associations
to Open To-Day in Denver
RECORD ATTENDANCE
Railroads, Packers and the
State Organizations Will
Have Delegates Present
DENVER, Jan. B.âEverything Is In
readiness for the opening to-morrow
of the Joint national convention of the
National Dive Stock Association and
the National Wool Growers' Associa
tion. Already delegate" are flocking
into Denver to attend the gatherings
and every hotel In the city is taxed al
most to its utmost to accommodate the
stockmen.
The wool growers will have the first
call in the proceedings. They will con
vene in the Tabor Grand Opera-house
to-morrow morning at 10 o'clock. Pres
ident W. E. Warren will deliver the
annual address in the morning and a
resolution for the appointment of a
committee to consider the proposed re
organization of the association will be
introduced.
This is one of the most Important
matters to come before the convention.
The wool growers will conclude their
business to-morrow so as to be able
to attend in a body the opening ses
sion of the National Live Stock As
sociation on Tuesday.
It is predicted that the attendance
at Tuesday's oDening session will
eclipse any previous annual meeting
of the organization and the officers say
the convention itself will prove the
most interesting and valuable to the
livestock world ever held.
Information received at association
headquarters is that all the stock
handling railroads and the leading
packers are sending representatives
and these, together with the delegates
from the State organization and lead
ing stock raisers, will bring the attend
ance to the neighborhood of 1000. The
convention proper will continue until
Thursday night. Friday and Saturday
will' be devoted to sightseeing.
TALK ABOUT
A DROLL MAN,
CRESSY'S IT
Wlil M. Cressy is with us again, and
the patrons of the Orpheum received
him with befitting demonstration last
night. Cressy's sketches are always
good, for there is something in his droll
humor, tempered as it is at unexpected
intervals with a promise of a bit of
pathos, that holds one in anticipation
of the climax with all that human con
centration can well afford to spare on
a one-act comedy. Blanche Dayne
lends Cressy an able support. "Town
Hall To-night" is the title of the sketch,
and it introduces Cressy as Hip Flitters,
a typical crossroad theatrical manager.
Miss Dayne, who represents Miss Gene
vieve Montmorency, arrives on the
scene as "leading lady of a traveling
troupe." The dialogue that develops
after her appearance at "Town Hall"
is cleverly maintained by Miss Dayne. !
It gives Cressy opportunity to dovetail !
into the situation his own inimitable
absurdities. It is sufficient to say that
Cressy's work was the hit of the even
ingâan evening of clever things at
that, which made it difficult to choose
between them from a standpoint of im- j
partialism.
Josle Kine and Phil Gotthold in "A j
Medical Discovery" were somewhat of ,
a disappointment, but their sketch did
apt admit of much good stage business, j
Winfield Douglas and Margie Ford j
caught the house with some pleasing I
songs and clever dancing, and Miss j
Nita Allen and Robert Eliott in "Wine, j
Women and Song" are well worth see- i
ing. Miss Allen is an actress of merit
and created something out of a very
superficial act. Eliott wears well
throughout the dialogue, and tha piece
was received with marked appreciation
by the Sunday-nighters.
H. V. Fitzgerald, lightning change
artist, was a trifle slow in his prelim
inary work, but before he had finished
his turn he had the house with him
from gallery to pit. Eleanor Falke is
as dainty and interesting as the show
bills have promised, and her voice is
good, too. Chassino's shadowgraphs
show what a man deft with fingers and
toes can do with a magic lantern and
a sheet. This act was distinctive and
introduced several entirely original
turns.
The four Bards, athletes, were as
wonderful as ever. The general opinion
Is that there are few In their particular
line of work who can equal them,

A new poetical drama, entitled
"Mizpah, or trie Story of Esther,"
will be produced for the first time in
this city at the Majestic Theater to
night. The play was writen by Ella
Wheeler Wilcox and Luscombe Sear
elle, the talented pair spending more
than two years on the beautiful piece.
The sqenes and stage settings are un
usually fine and fully illustrate the
strength and power of the drama. The
play has been carefully rehearsed for
several weeks and its Initial appear
ance is sure to be welcomed by the
theater-goers. Much is expected of
M Iss Eleanor Gordon, the Majestic's
talented star, who has undertaken the
task of appearing In the part of the
Queen. The music was written by Lus
combe Searelle and is said to be very
fine.

RAILWAY TIME CARDS
SOMETIMES CONFUSING
Passing From One Standard District
to Another Sets the Clock
Back.
An official of a railway running
through West "Virginia tells of an amus
i ing conversation between the conduc-
I tor of one of the company's trains
j running to Parkersburg and a pas
i senger, whose nervousness indicated
| that he was not accustomed to the
! vagaries of the train schedules.
Said the passenger: "Mr. Conduc
j tor, what time do we get into Parkers
burg?"
"Ten-twenty."
"Good. Now, at what hour can I get
a train there for the West?"
"Nine-twenty."
"To-morrow?"
"No. To-night."
At this the nervous passenger began
to evince signs of indignation. "Look
here, conductor," exclaimed he, "I'm
no fooi, you know. Cut out this jok
ing and tell me at what hour I can
get a train for the West."
"At nine-twenty to-night," reiterated
the imperturbable official, preparing
to pass on.
"Hold on a minute," grasped the
passenger, catching the conductor by
the sleeves. "Will you please tell me
how I am to leave Parkersburg one
hour before I arrive there?"
"Simply by keeping your seat and
not getting excited," was the calm
reply. "You'll get your train all
right."
The problem was too much for the
nervous passenger. He gazed help
lessly about him as if seeking light on
the subject from his fellow passengers.
Then, turning to the conductor, he ob
served :
"Now, old man, I haven't the least
doubt that you are a rattling good con
ductor and know your business from A
to Z: but I'll not admit that you can
work a reversing lever on the sun."
At this the conductor smiled. "I
don't mind telling you," he said, "that
the time changes at the river."
Whereupon all became clear to the
nervous passenger.
"Oh. I see!" he exclaimed, exultantly.
"At this rate, if I go on to San Fran
cisco, I'll get there some time last
week."âNew York Tribune.

enty-five persons remained in the bit
ing wind on the end of the dock watch
ing the tug in her efforts to get along

certain whether he could get off on the
Majestic. A friend was on the pier

This attracted the attention of some of
the officers, and he was told to get on
board quickly if he wanted to go.
"I am not going; I am waiting for
a friend and I do not kpow for certain
whether he is going." he said.
Three minutes after the Majestic had
left her dock and the friend had given
up his watch a cab containing Mr.
Smart arrived. He was assisted out
and his baggage thrown on a hand
truck. There was some quick time
made in getting to the end of the dock
end in signaling a tug. Shose who were
about to leave returned to the string
piece to watch the outcome of his ef
forts to continue Ins trip to Liverpool.
The vessel had pointed her nose down
stream when the tug, with her whistle
going, started after her. It was not
until the liner had reached the Bat
ten' that she consented to slow down
so that Mr. Smart and his baggage
could be hauled on board.

the gangplanks were being taKen
down, and It was only through the
courtesy of the superintendent, Mr.
Bennel. that, he was taken on board.

WHY WOMEN'S SHOE
LACES COME UNTIED
Shoe Salesman Gives Very Plausible
Explanation of the
Matter.
"Why is is that a lady's shoe be
comes untied so much more frequently
and apparently easier than a man's?"
repeated J. V. Ladd, the shoe sales
man, after me. "Well, I don't suppose
I would be in any better position than
any one else had it not been for the
fact that a few days ago a couple of
ladies, customers of my place, were
discussing that very point and their
deductions appear to solve the riddle
perfectly.
"It is not that a woman's shoe laces
will not tie in as firm a knot as any
other; the reason for the frequent an
noyances to which women are sub
jected is apart from that. In the case
of high shoes the trouble is altogether
in the height. The shoe laces farther
up on the leg than a man's, usually
fits more snugly, and therefore en
counters a greater strain on the knot
when a woman walking. The re
sult is that it becomes loosened within
a very short time, whereas a man may
walk all day without the laces of his
shoe becoming untied. Where low
shoes are worn the skirts flapping
around the ankle do the work of
loosening the knot."âSt. Louis Globe-
Democrat. â

GRATEFUL GIRL KISSES
OLD SENATOR CULLOM
Galesburg Miss Travels to Washington
ton to Keep Soldier Lover From
Philippines.
Senator Cullom received a hearty
kiss from a pretty girl in the room of
the Senate Committee on Foreign Re
lations recently.
The girl was Miss Minnie Swigert
of GalesbOrg, who had saved up $120
with which to purchase the freedom
of her sweetheart, John Pierce, who Is
a private in the Hospital Corps of the
United States army.
This money was deposited with the
proper authorities. Pierce looked for
ward to his discharge In time to marry
Miss Swigert during the holidays. He
was thunderstruck therefore the other
day when he received orders to leave
with his company for.the Philippines.
He wired the news to his sweetheart
and that young woman promptly came
all the way from Galesburg to Wash
ington to see Senator Cullom about it.
Miss Swigert went to the room of
the Committee on Foreign Relations,
interrupting Senator Cullom while he
was poring over the treaties pending
with foreign countries. She told him
her story and Senator Cullom took up
the telephone, rang up Surgeon Gen
eral O'Reilly and pleaded for the sol
dier lover.
"Don't send him to the Philippines,
general," pleaded Senator Cullom.
The girl's face was deadly pale and
she was intensely agitated. CUtlom
turned from the telephone and said:
"That matter is all arranged."
"What!" exclaimed the girl, jump
ing from her chair. "Isn't he going
to the Philippines?" N
"No, he'll stay at home."
"Oh, you dear old man," gasped the
maiden, leaping into Senator Cullom's
arms and giving him a hearty kiss.
"You dear, dear old man."
Then she ran from the room, it not
being long before Private John Pierce,
Hospital Corps, received a message
from the capitol telegraph office.
Senator Cullom turned to his secre
tary.
"Billy," he said, "I guess that's
enough for one day. We won't go
over those treaties any further." â

FIRST WEEK OF THE NEW TRADE YEAR.
WHILE general business is quiet, as usual during the first
half of January, conditions are visibly much better than at
this time last year. Then we were facing a recession in
trade, coupled with a conservatism full of timidity, with apprehen
. sions of a reaction. That reaction did appear and lasted for six
â  months, and it was not until June that the tide again turned toward
renewed activity, since which we have steadily gone upward, finally
closing the year at a slight advance over the preceding year. This
retrospect has already been dwelt upon in this column and needs
no further specification.
The features under which we enter upon 1905 are the bounteous
â¢crops of corn and cotton last year, increased railway earnings, light
stocks of almost all commodities, showing that production has not
â¢ yet overlapped consumption, continued expansion in the iron and
steel trades, the full employment of mill machinery, no idle labor
of any consequence and no serious labor controversies, improving
conditions in the winter wheat crop, which is looking very well, more
numerous arrivals of buyers in all the large markets, continued gains
in the country's bank clearings, fewer failures than at thÂ«Â£ time last
year, and none of them serious, enormous stocks of money with low
'â¢ . rates of interest, easy collections and a widespread feeling of opti
â¢ mism in strong contrast with the pessimism which existed at this
time last year. All these conditions point to good times during the
coming year. A few of them may vanish, but it is hardly possible
that all will; hence the confident predictions of the great bankers
â and financiers of the country that we are to enjoy another period
of prosperity seem to have a solid foundation.
The demand for all sorts of productions is certainly remarkable.
"Our mines are turning out immense quantities of gold, silver, iron
and copper to meet immediate requirements, and the three last
metals have lately made marked advancements in price. The condi
tion of the steel market is a good illustration of the state of the
metal trade. During the first half of 1904 the industry was so de
pressed that the production fell to the rate of 12,000,000 tons per
year, whereas it is now back again to the high water mark of 19,
000.000, or an increase of over 50 per cent. This is a most excellent
and encouraging exhibit and speaks #ell for the future. We are
now producing pig iron faster than Great Britain, France and Ger
many combined, while our copper production is at least 10 per cent
. larger than last year. Nor is this all. Iron, copper, tin, lead, rubber,
wool, drugs, chemicals and a long list of farm and factory staples
are selling considerably higher than a year ago, simply because con
' sumption is still ahead of production, and as long as this condition
lasts times cannot be otherwise than active and good. The only great
staple selling lower than a year ago is cotton, which has declined to
the cost of production and even below it, under what is probably the
largest crop ever known.
On December 1 the supply of money in the United States was
$2,573,000,000, against $2,449,000,000 on the same date the preceding
\-ear. This is a pretty fair bank account to contemplate. Another
good condition which all sensible citizens will indorse is that during
1904 the total industrial consolidations, as closely as could be esti
' mated, aggregated only about $185,000,000, against $425,000,000 in
1903. $1,122,000,000 in 1902 and $2,805,000,000 in 1901. Evidently
the "trust mania" is waning, as far-seeing financiers predicted it
would as far back as 1901, even while it was at its most acute stage,
as shown by the above figures. Every evil, whatever may be its
character, contains within itself the germs of its own destruction.
. which fructify and destroy it when it has reached its limit. This is
the law of the universe and it cannot be evaded. Every habitual
malefactor eventually lands in jail somewhere or other.
.Another point: The net result of our foreign commerce during
â¢ the first eleven months of 1904 was an excess of exports amounting
â¢ â  to $366,000,000, which has never been exceeded, except in 1903, when
'. : the balance in our favor reached $392,000,000 for the same period.
â¢ These figures speak for themselves.
The above enumerations show in a broad and general way the
â  commercial and financial condition of the United States. It was
.. never better. We have never yet gone into a new year with fairer
prospects. What may happen later on no man can foresee; but up to
date we are still on the crest of the wave of prosperity.
THE SOUYTH'S FIGHT FOR LAW.
IN the current number of McClure's Magazine Ray Stannard Baker
contributes an article upon "Lynching in the South," which by
its impartiality of spirit and soundness of deductions commends
.itself to thinking people. At present time, when the public officials,
the press and the people of the whole South are making a desperate
stand against the excesses of mob rule, a review of the situation such
as that set forth by this writer cannot but call attention to the diffi
culty of the problem facing the people of the South and emphasize
the progress they have already made. â¦
Mr. Baker reflects the sentiment of the Southerner who would
countenance a lynching even while deprecating the violation of the
law. The fault lies partly with the negro himself and partly with
the courts of justice according to this authority. In the far South
there is a class of the colored race, little known to the North and
never seen in the Westâthe worthless, vagrant negro; he it is that
shuns work, prowls the roads for plunder, listens often to the in
cendiary harangues of colored preachers, and hesitates not to give
himself over to the commission of the unspeakable crimes that are
so often the motive for a lynching. This negro is shunned by the
better class of his own race and treated by the whites in sparsely set
tled districts as a predatory animal, to be feared. There is no race
prejudice to act as inspiration for retaliation on the part of the
whites against lawless negroes of this class; it is a question of pro
tection purely.
A general distrust of the efficacy of courts finds deep lodgment
iij the Southerner's convictions according to Baker. Particularizing,
the writer says: "The Alabama system of justice is cursed by the
professional juror, chosen by politicians and often open to political
influences. This, with the unlimited right of appeal and the great
.number of peremptory challenges allowed to the defense in accepting
jurymen, gives such power to the lawyers for the defendent that con
victions are exceedingly difficult." As a result of this laxity the
feeling is prevalent that somehow the lawyers will cheat justice and
the sentiment of an outraged community is too likely to forestall
the.impotency of the courts by mob justice.
But with the closer knitting together of the sparsely settled dis
tricts and with the growing unanimity of spirit among those whose
pride of State cannot tolerate further lawlessness the lynching prob
lem in the South is rapidly finding its solution. And, as Mr. Baker
trenchantly says, "The South has no lessons to learn from the North
in so far as the lynching problem is concerned." The South, he as
sures us, in making fully as creditable progress against its special
evils of lawlessness as the North. It is not incumbent upon those
above Mason and Dixon's line to draw any pharisaical comparisons.
WORD comes from Stockton that the Chamber of Commerce
of that city, now entering upon the fifth year of its activities,
purposes raising its membership of 500 to 1000 in order that
the rapidly broadening interests of the city of the San Jdaquin may
receive adequate direction and promotion at the hands of its cor
' porate businessmen. This enterprise on the part of the Chamber
of Commerce is specially commendable at this time, when the wave
of prosperity is at the flood throughout the whole State. What with
the plans for flood lands reclamation and the increased facilities for
river transportation that will accrue therefrom, Stockton seems to
have a sure position in the broad thoroughfare of progress and its
Chamber of Commerce is assured plenty of employment for the
future.
ENTERPRISE IN STOCKTON.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 9, 1905.
The Moon's Craters and
What Then Send
Forth in Eruption
IF there be craters on the moon that
are anything- but extinct, they must
expel something. Judged by the
discharges of our earthly vol
canoes, that something must be
water and carbonic acia gas. Water
cannot possibly exist as a liquid; for
the temperature of the moon's surface
during the long lunar night is probably
not far from 460 degrees below the zero
mark of a Fahrenheit thermometer.
Ice and snow are the forms, then,
which lunar water must assume. Is
there any evidence of it? Hundreds
of "craterlets" are lined with a silvery
coating that gleams dazzlingly when
the sun shines full upon them. Cap
ping the loftier peaks the same silver
glow may be seen. On tlie slopes of
the greater mountain chains, on the
ramparts of huge craters the silvery
sheen casts its halo, fading away
strangely as the sun rises higher and
higher, and reappearing at sunset just
before the long, cold lunar night sets
in. From many of the craters, notably
from Tycho, long white rays spread
out for hundreds of milesâenigmas in
the old moon of a generation ago, but
in the new moon of to-day deep
crevices illuminated only when the sun
is high in the lunar heavens. What
is this silvery substance that caps the
Apennines of the moon, gleams on the
slopes and radiates from the craters?
According to the new school it is
simply ice and snow, collecting at the
poles, on summits, and in the very
places where it ought to collect. More
over, It partly explains the curious
changes that occur at different times
of the lunar day in the size of the
crater Linne, the "very dmall, round,
brilliant spot," previously mentioned;
it explains the illumination of deep,
snow-bottomed pits and abysses that
are inky black at sunrise and sunset,
and brilliantly white when the sun
shines directly into them; and it ex
plains the fading away and reappear
ance of white stains at different
periods in the lunar day. The melting
and falling of snow, the disappearance
and reappearance of hoar frost, alone
can account for these changes. In old
descriptions they are said to be due to
variations in illumination; In the phil
osophy of the new moon they are
attributed with beautiful simplicity to
the alternate evaporation and freezing
of water expelled from craters, in
eruption.âWaldemar B. Kaempffert in
the December Booklovers

Fraud in Gun -Barrels.
In his annual report on the trade of
Belgium Consul General Hertslet
remarks that a large quantity of gun
barrels are sent annually from Liege
to Birmingham to be tested, in order
that they may have the Birmingham
mark. They are then sent back to be
finished and made up at Liege, and
are frequently sold as British guns.
This would be avoided, says the En
gineer of London, if all gun barrels
manufactured at Liege were marked
"Made in Belgium" before being ac
cepted for proof in Birmingham.

CAPTAIN'S BRAVE LITTLE WIFE
AIDS CREW OF SINKING SHIP
IT was the encouragement of a brave little woman that
kept the almost demoralized crew of the British bark
entine Stewiacke from deserting their post at the
pumps when the vessel filled with water and drifted
for five days in the Atlantic Ocean.
The heroine was Mrs. Mabel Airey, wife of the Stew
iacke's captain, and she gave her account of the thrilling
experiences through which she passed to a Philadelphia
Inquirer reporter while seated in the cabin of the Aus
trian steamer Lucia, which took Mrs. Airey with the crew
from the sinking vessel and brought them to port.
Mrs. Airey is but a slip of a woman, with blue eyes,
light hair, and small, regular features. She seems no more
than 28 years old. She has accompanied her.husband on
many voyages and she can keep a logbook and use nau
tical terms with the facility of an old salt. She said:
"We left NoVn Scoha for Eurore in September We
took on a cargo of salt at Cadis, Spain, and left thore f<>r
Yarmouth. Nova Scotia, on November 1". Besides my
husband and myself, we had on board the Stewuv-ke, the
mate, the cook, thÂ«; boatswain and five able seamen. We
had fair weather for ten days, but on Sunday, November
2~, we encountered a terrific gale. The vessel strained
under the battering of the waves and before long we
found that some ti the sef.ms had opened and fourteen
inches of water was cominer in every hour.
"The captain ordered the men to the pumps, and from
that time until Friday afternoon, December 2, when we
sighted the Lucia, only one man was permitted to leave
the pumps at a time to rest. In the five days my husband
slept but a half hour. Water poured in fore and aft and
the messroom was washed out completely. We were then
al cut 2( 0 mib s norn the nearest land, St. Marys island,
one of the Azores.
"My husband ordered the oil bags thrown over, and it
was this precaution that saved us, for the oil greater re
duced the force if the waves. About 9 o'clock at night thi
waves carried away the wheel ropes. Fortunately the
captain was at the wheel and was able to hold it while

The Hon. John Sharp Williams,
leader of the minority in the House of
Representatives, says that one day
while leisurely driving down a road
near his home town in Mississippi he
observed a darky reclining under a

gazing lazily up through the branches
of the tree, and a hoe lay beside him.
In the cornfield adjoining the road
there could be seen. Mr. Williams
states, many weeds impeding the
growth of the grain.
"What are you doing there, Sam?"
asked Mr. Williams.
"I'se heah to hoe dat corn, sah," was
the answer.

"Not exactly, sah. I ain't hardly

In' fo' de sun to go down, so I kin auit

When Bad Coin Is Lawful.
In China spurious coin may be law
fully manufactured when It is intend
ed to be placed in the coffins of the
dead. The Chinese believe that these
bad coins make the dead happy.

MRS. MABEL AIREY.
one of the seamen brought fresh ropes, or we would have
all gone to the bottom.
"On Monday the sailors came to my husband and asked
him to put into the nearest port. There was three feet
of water in the hold. The men seemed demoralized and
I did what I could to cheer them. I told them that I
would take a hand at the pumps if it became necessary.
"They went back to the pumps and worked steadily
through the storm. On Tuesday they again asked my
husband to head for the nearest port. He said it was
useless. In the afternoon they again asked him and he
turned the bow of the vessel toward the Canary Islands.
But the wind blew the vessel out of the course and the
sailors saw that it was impossible to reach land. They
told my husband that as soon as a vessel was sighted they
would refuse to go on duty, and he promised to abandon
the Stewiacke as soon as a ship came in view.
"For three days more we tossed about in the Atlantic.
The sailors were exhausted from the long strain and lack
of sleep. I encouraged them all I could, but they were
on the point of giving up. We were all resigned to die,
for we felt sure that no help would reach us In time.
The head of the vessel was almost under the sea and
there were five feet of water in the hold. On Friday
afternoon Seaman Neils Jacobson sighted the Lucia. We
raised distress signals and we feared that the vessel would
not see us.' But we soon saw it coming toward us. We
launched a boat and sent all hands, with the exception of
my husband, the boatswain and myself, to the Lucia.
"The boat returned for us and I packed up some of
my belongings. Then we abandoned the Stewiacke. Cap
tain Zacevich has been most kind to us, supplying our
every want. He gave us the best he had on his vessel."
Although Mrs. Airey modestly referred to her part in
keeping up the spirits of the crew, they declared that
they would have given up hope and gone to the bottom
of the sea if it had not been for her smiles and encour
agement. Wilfrid Powell, the British Consul, took charge
of Captain Airey, his wife and the crew, and arranged
to send them to Nova Scotia.
BABY'S GRAMMAR.
I know a young grammarian.
She's only three feet high,
And yet she conjugates a verb
With far more sense than I.
For when I asked her, "Did you go?**
Her words by logic sent
Are heard in answer: "Yes, I goed."
(She never says "I went.")
She's only four, yet with the verbs
She never has a doubt;
The ones that have a crooked twist
She promptly straightens out.
And rules the grammar sharps revere
Get shocks both swift and rudeâ
She never, never says "I did."
She always says "I doed."
With be and was and being been,
With see and saw and seen,
With go and went and going gone,
With meaning meant and meanâ
I often wish that at the start
Some four-year-old Hortense
Had goed to making grammar rules
And doed the thing with sense!

"DON'T COURT IN DARK"
"Courtships should not continue
longer than seven or eight months.
Beyond that they are dangerous."
So declared the Rev. Father Angelo
Rauber in an address to the alumnae
of St. Xavier's Academy at Latrobe,
Pa.
Father Angelo said the girl of to-day
has so much freedom that there is con
stant danger for her. He does not ap
prove of the family deserting the parlor
when the young man calls, nor would
he permit of dark corners or dim par
lors.
"The hours for calling." he says,
"should be from 8:30 to 10:30 o'clock in
the evening, and with no additional
half-hours to say good-by. Young folks
should not sit close on the sofa, so en
grossed in their happiness that they
forget the flight of time."
Waterfalls to Run Siberian Road.
Count Lubienski, a Russian engineer,
has brought forward a proposal for the
electrification of the Trans-Siberian
Railway. The numerous waterfalls In
the vicinity of the line might, he
thinks, be utilized to give a current
of 100,000 volts.

Rich Woman and Her
Beautiful Daughter
to Enter a Convent
AFTER careful consideration cov
ering a period of several years
Mme. Constance Mahlman and
her accomplished daughter, Ma
rie, of Grymes Hill, Tompkinsville, Sta
ten Island, have decided to renounce
the world and devote the rest of their
lives to the Roman Catholic church.
Mother and daughter will enter a con
vent in the spring.
Mme. Mahlman has been a resident
of Staten Island for many years, and
her large estate on Grymes Hill is one
of the beauty snots of Staten Island.
Four years ago Mme. Mahlman sur
prised her numerous friends by donat
ing a large parcel of land adjoining her
home to the Sisters of the Visitation, a
cloistered order. The sisters had a con
vent at Jamaica, L.. 1., but at once be
gan the erection of a convent on the
property deeded to them by Mme. Mahl
man. Mme. Mahlman at that time ex
pressed her intention of entering tha
convent with her daughter, but certain
formalities had to be gone through and
she abandoned the idea for the time.
The church and convent were partly
completed when the sisters found that
being a cloistered order they would bo
unable to maintain themselves and
abandoned the work of erecting th<;
buildings. The property was returmd
to Mme. Mahlman.
At this time the Rev. H. J. Murphy.
O. S. A., of Villanova College, Phila
delphia, was looking for a place to es
tablish a church and college of the
Augustinian order and selected this
property. He purchased the land and
half-completed buildings from Mme.
Mahlman and soon completed the build
ings.
Mme. Mahlman is a member of the
German nobility and Is in receipt of a
large Income from her estates in Ger
many. Her beautiful daughter, Marie,
has shared with her mother her devo
tion to religion and was rarely seen in
society. Mme. Mahlman is a strikingly
handsome woman of about 50 years.
Her daughter is about 25 years old.
Two years ago burglars entered their
home and after chloroforming all the
inmates ransacked the house. Mme.
Mahlman never fully recovered from
the shock and has been ill at intervals
since. It was her intention to enter the
convent several months ago, but she
suffered an attack of pneumonia and
was obliged to postpone her entry. She
is now recovering and hopes to be able
to carry out her plans early in the
spring.
It is understood that she will turn
over her fortune to the church. Her
mansion on Grymes Hill Is a valuable
property. Since the burglary she has
had armed watchmen stationed about
the place at night and visitors are
warned away. Mother and daughter
are rarely seen in the streets, although
both are regular attendants at the
Church of Our Lady of Good Counsel,
which is less than 100 feet from their
home.

ELECTRIC RAILWAYâN., City.
The first application of electricity as a
motive power was by Thomas Daven
port of Brandon, Vt., in 1835, when he
operated cars on a circular track. In
April, 1851, Professor Pagh of the
Smithsonian Institution operated an
electric road over the tracks of the
Baltimore and Washington Railway.
The speed attained was nineteen miles
an hour.

An excellent programme was pre
sented before the department of edu
cation of the California Club at its
regular monthly meeting on Friday
afternoon at the club rooms, Madame
Tojetti, chairman of the department,
1 presiding.
â¢ Frank Bunker of the San Francisco
Normal School was the first speaker,
his subject being "Library Work in the
Public Schools." He told a number of
stories illustrating the need of broader
reading among the teachers and the
pupils, advocating the use of geograph
ical readers in connection with geogra
phy.
Mrs. Angelo of the California Club
spoke on the subject from the club wo
man's point of view. She made an ex
cellent practical address, advising her
hearers to find out what had already
been done by schools and libraries be
fore making plans for work. It was
quite evident that she had already "act
ed upon this advice herself, as she was
ready with references to school and
library work in cities East and West,
with statistics illustrating her points.
She said there should be a permanent
reference library in every school; that
teachers' privileges in the public libra
ries should be extended; that librari
ans should visit the schools, and that
classes should be taken to the libraries
and instructed in the use of references
and of indexes.
Mr. Lichtenstein of the San Francisco
Public Library presented the subject
from the librarian's standpoint. He
said that the chief difficulty in the way
of extending the use of library books
was that of transportation, there be
ing no way of carrying large bundles
or boxes of bookd from library to
school and return except at the teach
ers' own trouble and expense.
The last speaker was Mr. Kimball,
who had visited many libraries and
had a fund of valuable information on
modern library work. He spoke brief
ly, as the hour was late. The entire
programme was full of suggestions of
progressive work that will be under
taken by the California Club.

TIM SULLIVAN
HERE FOR REST
New York's Famous Politi
cian Is Paying His First
Visit to San Francisco
â¢
"PRY DOLLAR'S" NEPHEW
Young Alderman Swings a
Mighty Influence in the
Big Eastern Metropolis
Timothy Sullivan, a member of New
York City's Board of Aldermen, who
shares with his famous uncle, Timothy
"Dry Dollar" Sullivan, a reputation of
being, one of the important political
factors of the big Eastern metropolis,
has been a guest at the Palace Hotel
for several days. With him is Senator
Anderson of New York, who enjoys the
distinction of being one of the few
Democrats elected in that city at the
last election.
"Little Tim" Sullivan, as his political
constituents in New York designate
him, is paying his first visit to San
Francisco and says he is heartily en
joying himself. He is here oh pleasure
and will remain for several days, tak
ing a rest after the labors of a hard
and not particularly gratifying national
campaign. In New York it is said that
"Little Tim" controls more votes than
any other man in the great republic,
his influence extending throughout the
territory from Twenty-third street to
the Bowery. He began his career on
the streets of New York, rising from a
bootblack. Although assisted by his
uncle to gain a foothold in politios,
there are many who say that "Little
Tim" would have succeeded without
that aidr and they go even further by
saying that the nephew's shrewdness
and greater amount of intelligence have
materially helped the elder Sullivan in
his own political projects during the
last few years, and also assisted him in
making successful his many private
business enterprises, which Include rac
ing ventures, pugilistic events, saloons,
hotels and other propositions, that are
steadily swelling the bank account of
"Big Tim" Sullivan.

SENATE EXPECTS FIGHT
_âÂ»
An Attempt Will Be Made to I
Hold Up the Appointment
of Two Supreme Judges
DENVER, Jan. B.âA hot fight is
â  expected in the Senate when the
; Judges of the Supreme Court appolnt
'ed yesterday by Governor Peabody
! come up for confirmation. The Demo
[ crats held a caucus to-night and at its
conclusion declared that they would
fight the nominations to the Supreme
i bench of Bailey and Goddard with all
! their power. They claim, however,
j that with the Republican Senators,
j Campbell and Delong, voting with
( them they will be able to prevent the
confirmation.
The Republican leaders declared
this afternoon that they had received
personal pledges from enough Repub
lican Senators to insure the confirma
tion of the two men. They were not,
however, overconfident of the issue
and admitted that there would be
something of a fight before the con
firmation was made.
It is Governor Peabody's present in
tention to contest the election of Gov
ernor-elect Adams. Under the law he
cannot file notice of a contest before
the sixth day of the session, which will
be to-morrow. He has, however, sev
eral days in which to file his notice of
contest and he may not do so until
after the Inauguration of Governor
elect Adams.
Adams said to-day:
I do not know whether Governor Peabody
will make a contest or not, but I expect that
he will. I am in a much better position for
such a proceeding now than I was two days
ago. At that time the â¢ investigation was con
fined by the oourt to the city and county of
Denver. Now it includes the whole Stats, and
I feel confident that the Democrats can show
as many fraudulent Republican votes in the
outlying counties as the Republicans) claim
| were cast by the Democrats in Denver.

for Nurses will be held at the hospital on

the purpose of electing a board of directors
for the ensuing year and for any other busi
ness that may come before the meeting.

THE California Debris Commission, having
received applications to mine by hydraulic
process from F. C. Barnum In American
House mine, near American House, Plumas
County, Cel., draining into dry ravine which
reaches Valley Creek and South Fork of
Feather River, and from Harry Johns and
C. F. Cowan, in Spring Hill Mine, near So
nora, Tuolumne County, Cal., draining into
Woods Creek, which reaches Tuolumne Riv
er, gives notice that a meeting to receive
any protests Will be held at room 68, Flood
building, San Francisco, Cal.. Jan. 23, 1906.
at 1:80 p. m.
THE California Debris Commission, having re
ceived applications to mine by hydraulic pro
cess from Wlnr Kee, in American Hydraulic
mine, near Port Wins, Sierra County, Cal.,
draining Into Morrlstown Ravine, thence Into
Yuba River; from Jerry Curtis, In Forest
Grove mine, near Johnsville, Plumas County,
Cat, draining iÂ»to East Nelson Creek, which
reaches Feather River; from North Star Min
ing Company In Lancell Placer mine, near
Mokelumne Hill. Calaveras County, Cal.,
draining into Rich Gulch, which reaches
Calaveras River; and from J. A. Hartman,
In Clayton Ranch Ravine mine, near Forbee
town, Yuba County, Cal., draining into Dry
Creek, which reaches Yuba River, gives no
tice that a meeting to receive any protests
will be held at room 68. Flood building,
San Francisco, Cal.. January 16, 1905, at
1:80 p. m.

FOR STRIKING CHINESE
Case Against Redding Yonths Who
Assaulted Celestial After Taking
His Liquor Is Dismissed.
REDDING, Jan. B.âThe four prom
inent young men charged with rob
bing a Chinese merchant at Shasta
have beer discharged after a prelim
inary examination. The Chinese said
they came to his place of business at
night, and when he opened thy: door
in response to their knocks felled him
T-ith a blow and stole money and
liquor from the store.
Murray, Read, Gilbert and Bosweli,
the defendants, whose prominence ftt
tracted much attention /to the case,
said they went to the store somewhat
under the influence of liquor and, af
ter getting the Chinese to the door,
took a bottle of whisky and told him
to charge It. The storekeeper object
ed and thÂ«y knocked him do-*n.
A bloodhound hunt club, which has

days over a line of "natural hunting
country" about 100 miles from Lon
don. Lord Linlithgow is president of
the club.
STEAMER LAKME IS SORRY
SIGHT IN DRYDOCK.
SHOWS,EVIDENCE OF
CONTACT WITH SHOALS
ââ. i
Will Cost $15,000 to Fit Her For Sea
The steam schooner Lakme as she
lies in*he big- drydock at Hunters Point
is a sorry looking spectacle. Her
keel is smashed, her rudder Dost
is broken, she has two great holes
In her** starboard side, her propel
ler is twisted and altogether she is
one of the most demoralized looking
objects that has ever been towed Into
this port, t
As soon as she arrived here she
was towed up to Hunters Point
and an attempt was made to put her
into the small drydock. As she was
drawing twenty-five feet this was
found impossible, so the big dock was
opened up and the practically sub
merged vessel was floated in there. In
this dock, which is 760 feet long, she
occupies but a little corner. Captain
A. F. Pillsbury, surveyor for the San
Francisco board of underwriters, made
an examination yesterday. He thinks the
cost of repairing the vessel will be
about $15,000. Her valuation is placed
at about $30,000. When she left Eureka
she had a cargo of about 600,000 feet of
lumber stored on her decks and in her
hold.
The first great sea that washed
over her after she bumped over the
shoals at the Eureka bar washed away
her whole deckload. When she got out
Into the open In her practically dis
mantled condition the seas thundered
away upon her decks until they dis
lodged her hatches, and the lumber and
shingles began floating out Into the sea.
Out of the original cargo of 600,000 feet
of lumber that she started with but
100,000 feet remain in her hold. Her
troubles did not end with her arrival at
the drydock. She had barely floated In
and was about settling down to the
blocks, which were being adjusted by
Diver Abrahamson, when she suddenly
toppled over to one side. The dock was
again flooded, and she was riarhted.
Catches Foot in Cartrack.
H. A. Parker was landed at the Harbor
Hospital at 3 o'clock yesterday morning with
both bones of hia right leg broken. How it
happened he was not exactly sure, but he said
he thought he caught his foot in the street ear
track, which threw him down to the pavement.
He said he was a prominent member of the
Foresters. Later, at his request, he was taken
to the City and County Hospital.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
A depression of small area overlies Nevada
and Central California and has caused rain In
the Sacramento Valley and central portion of
the State, and cloudy and threatening, weather
over Southern California and Arizona. Fair
weather prevails over the northern portion of
thed Pacific Slope.
The changes in both 1 temperature and pres
sure have been slight In all districts.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours, ending midnight. January 9:
California, north of Tehaohap!âClearing In
north portion; showers In south portion Mon
day; light northwest wind.
California, south of TehachapiâShowera
Monday; light west wind.
Nevada âFair, except rain or snow In the
Sierras and south portion Monday.
San Francisco and vicinityâShowers Mon
day; light northwest wind.
Log Angeles and vicinityâShowers Mon
day; light west wind.
Sacramento and vicinityâClearing Monday;
light north wind.
Fresno and vicinityâShowers Monday; light
north wind.

the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence aa to time; the
fourth time column gives the last tide of the
day except when there are but three tides, as
sometimes occurs. The heights given are in
addition to the soundings of ths United States
Coast Survey Charts, except when a minus (â)
sign precedes the height, and then the number
given is subtracted from the depth given by
the charts. The plane of reference is the mean
of the lower low waters.

IMPROVEMENT SHOWN
BY BISHOP SPALDING
Has Regained the Use of His Left Arm
and Speaks With More
Freedom.
CINCINNATI, Jan. B.âThere was a
noticeable improvement to-night in
the condition of Bishop John L, Spald
ing, the Catholic prelate, who was
suddenly strloken with paralysis at
his residence on Friday afternoon. He
has regained somewhat the use of his
left arm and speaks with more free
dom. The remainder of the left side
of his body, however, he is unable to
use at all.
Drs. Spalding and Sla-vin to-night
spoke in a hopeful tone, but are
watching the patient closely for any
sign of a setback, which they admit
would portend the most serious re
sults.

, Â«$l per Year. .SPORTSMEN GATHER AT BAKERSFIELD WITH POINTERS AND SETTERS

Blooded Setters and Pointers Matched to

NO SYMPATHY
FOR LAWRENCE
Los Angeles Baeegoers Are
All Sore on Jockey and
Want to See Him No More
Special Dlscatch to The Call.
LOS ANGELES, Jan. B.âLast week
was a notable one in racing circles in
Los Angeles, and it is safe to say that
if there are as many developments at
Ascot this week the sports will think
that they are fast coming into their
â own in the way of getting an even
. break.
The most commendable thine: that
the judges did was the suspension of
, Jockey Lawrence, and for the eood of
âthe game it is to be hoped that he will
be ruled off. As far as any one has
been able to observe he has done the
game no good since he has been riding
here, and he has ridden any number
of races that appear to be very peculiar
ones. Just why he should be reinstated
cannot be made apparent, and he is
bound t" put the sport under suspicion
if he is put back. Lawrence is not the
only boy at Ascot that should be at
tended to, arid without doubt a num
ber of others will be reached before
many days.
During the week two trainers were
suspended, and no less than four horses
and the owner of another was fined $50
for Bending his horse into a race in an
â¢ unfit condition.
Hayes Valley Club Bouts.
Manager Morris Levy has prepared
a gre;a boxing card for next Friday
.night and expects to crowd Wooward's
Pavilion. Lew Powell, the light
weight champion, will meet Young
Choynski: ".South Side" Jack O'Brien
will meet a hard man in Abe Label
and Frank Mayfield will battle with
Joe Thomas for the welter-weight
championship of the coast. Besides
these bouts four pairs of boxers well
â¢known in local fistic circles will scrap
for trophies.
FANCY DRESS BALL CAUSES
THE SPREAD OF SMALLPOX
Theaters. Schools and Churches in
Billings, .Mont., dosed to Check
the Disease.
BILLINGS, Mont., Jan. B.âThe
ajarming spread of smallpox in this
city within the past forty-eight hours
has caused the authorities to adopt
the most stringent measures to blot
out the disease. Upon the order of
the Board of Health, ail church gath
erings have been abandoned, theatri
cal engagements canceled and the
schools closed. Lodge gatherings are
also- prohibited.
More than thirty cases of smallpox
have been reported to the officials
within the past two days, though as
yet the disease does not appear to be
of a malignant character. The pest
house is filled to overflowing and nu
merous residences are being quaran
tined to-day.
Mayor Foster, in a statement to
day, declares the prevalence of the
disease is directly due to a fancy ball
given by a secret organization in Bill
ings about two weeks ago, at which
a number of costumes imported from
an Eastern city were used. The Mayor
states that all of the afflicted ones
were present at that ball. j
ACCUSED OF ORGANIZING
REVOLUTIONARY TRIBUNAL
Officers and Clergy of Usknb, in Euro
pean Turkey, Placed Under
Arrest.
USKUB, European Turkey, Jan. 8.
On December 28. by order of Hilmi
Pasha, the municipal officers of Uskub,
together with the vicar and clergy of
the vicaret, were arrested, the accusa
tion being that they had formed a
revolutionary tribunal, i A search of
the homes of the accused was without
result, but all efforts to secure their
liberation have been unsuccessful.

ico, devastating a strip of fine timber

TWO LIKELY DOGS WHICH WILL
COMPETE IN FIELD TRIALS AT
BAKERSFIELD.
Special Dispatch to The CalL
BAKERSFIELD, Jan. B.âSports
men from all parts of the State have
been gathering here for several days
to participate in the twenty-second an
nual trials of the Pacific Coast Field
Trials Club. The first brace of dogs
ill the Derby stakes will be set down
to-morrow morning upon the Gosford
ranch, near this city.
Secretary Albert Betz went over the
preserved ground of the club to-day
and found the quail plentiful and ail
other conditions favorable. There has
been a slight rainfall this evening, but
the handlers of the dogs do not expect
this to interfere with the trials. It
will improve conditions if the rain
does not continue throughout the
night. It will take two days to run
the Derby, after which the All-age
stake will be taken up. The purse in
the Derby will be $510 and a trophy
for the dogs finishing first, second and
third.
Out of the original thirty-nine
nominations for the Derby twenty
nine paid second forfeit and seventeen
will start, as against twenty last year.
The draw to-night resulted as follows:
J. W. Flynn's Nellie vs. Stockdale Kennels'
Checkmate; J. W. Flynn's Romp vs. W, W.
Van Arsdale's Modesto; J. E. Terry's "Romp vs.

Kennels' Japan vs J. W. Flynn's Burbank;
W. W. Van Arsdale's Mariposa vs. J. E.
Terry's Kenwood; Rose St Christensen's Del
Rey Belle vs. M. Lawrence's Youley; J. E.
Terry's Rival vs. Charles Courts' Bolin's Duke;
J. E. Terry's Lady Like vs. A. C. Cadogan's
Chiqulte; Mountain View Kennels' Fandango
a bye.
Among the well-known sportsmen
now here are:
President W. W. Van Arsdale Secretary Al
bert Betz, H. L. Betten J. W. Flynn. J. K.
Terry. J. M. Kllgarif. H. W. Keller. J. B.
Haver, J. H. Schumacher. William Dormer.
H. T. Payne Charles Babcock E. H. Buggs
of Saginaw. Mich., E. H. Cox. J. W. Creach.
W. B. Coutts. R. M. Dodge. J. E. Lucan,
Charles Coutts. Fred Coutts, E. Valencia. S.
M. Carlisle, L. J. Rose and two sons, Judge
C. N. Poet. S, Christensen.
Seventeen crack setters and pointers
will meet in the Derby stakes at
Bakerslield.
TWO DOZEN PERSONS KILLED
BY BLIZZARD IN THE ALPS
Great Havoc Caused by Storm in Eu
ropeâCasualties Off the
English Coast.
GENEVA, Jan. B.âlt is said that
twenty-four persons lost their lives in
the mountains during the recent bliz
zard, fourteen of the casualties being
on the St. Bernard pass.
LONDON, Jan. B.âStormy weather
still continues on the British coasts
and several shipping casualties are re
ported. The Glasgow steamship Stella
Maris collided off Holyhead with the
Spanish vessel Oris and both sank
The crews were saved in the boats
after drifting all night.
The Belfast schooner Dispatch col
lided with the Sunderland' steamship
Dillington off Ramsgate this morning.
The Dispatch was towed inta Rams
gate, but the Dillington is believed to
have sunk with her crew of ten men.
Several other vessels were driven
ashore at different points, their crews
being rescued with great difficulty.
NEW ORDERS FAIL TO GIVE
NEW YORK A DRY SUNDAY
Despite Jerome's Crusade the Situa
tion Remains the Same as It
Has Been.
NEW YORK, Jan. B.âlt was by no
means a dry Sunday in Manhattan to
day. District Attorney Jerome's cru
sade for the strict enforcement of the
excise law and Magistrate Onmen's
order that the side doors of all saloons
must be kept closed and all inside par
titions tending to obstruct the view reÂ«
moved had the effect of stirring the
police to unusual activity, and many
arrests were made, but on the whole
the situation was much the same as
it had teen on preceding Sundays.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALU MONDAY, JANUARY 9, 1905.
HERFORD SEEKS
A GO FOR GANS
Baltimore Manager and His
Charge Arrive to Engage
Britt or Some Other Lad
â¢
Al Herford and his fighting machine,
Joe Gans. quietly slipped up Market
street last night, and in a short time
the entire sporting fraternity was
aware that the pair' had arrived safely.
Herford says they came to meet Jimmy
Gardner, but those who know are sure
that the wise man from Baltimore town
yearns to sign his black wonder against
Jimmy Britt for a return match.
Herford has much to say about Gans*
Inability to make 133 pounds at ring
side when he faced the Californian
here last October. He feels confident,
however, that 133 pounds at 6 o'clock
will be like a cake-eating contest for
his boy, and he 'boldly announces his
willingness to pit him against any like
ly fighter here at this mark.
If no match can be arranged in this
city Herford says he is willing that the
pair meet in Baltimore for a $20,000
purse. Herford announces his willing
ness to guarantee this amount, and
adds he will be. satisfied with any good
referee, no matter whether he is a San
Francisco man br fresh from the corn
fields of the Missouri Valley.
It looks as if the Baltimore boxer is
destined to meet Mr. Britt again next
month. Herford surely has something
up his sleeve, for he is going to anchor
here tor two weqjks, and a wise man
can do a deal of business In that time.
Herford does not say outright that he
is hot after Britt, but he undoubted
ly is.
More than likely Britt and Herford
will hold a quiet little "confab" within
the next few days and then the matter
will be thoroughly threshed out and
plans outlined for the match, which the
man from Baltimore has been thinking
about for many moons. Just what
stand Britt will take remains to be
seen, but if things look right James
cannot afford to keep idle.
Herford boldly says he will not let
Britt off with such generous terms as
he had when the articles were signed
for the last go. If Nelson or Corbett
wants to take a chance against the
dusky man Herford expresses his will
ingness to talk business with them.
At all events, indications point to a
mill in this city soon in which the
lightweight champion will play a prom
inent part.
VAMPIRES PLAY RINGS
AROUND OCCIDENTALS
Albion Rovers Roll Up a Bis; Score
Against Eagles on Sloppy
Field.
Three matches for the champion
ship of the California Association
Football League were scheduled for
yesterday, but only two were played.
The Oakland Hornets were to have
played the Independents at Idora
Park, Oakland. The Independents
claimed the match by default, as the
Hornets did not show.
On the Alameda cricket ground the
Vampires beat the Occidentals 4
goals to 2. In the first period the
Vampires scored 2 goals to their oppo
nent's 1 and in the second period they
added 2 more, while the Occidentals
made L
At Freeman's Park, Golden Gate,
the Albion Rovers beat the Eagles 8
goals to 0. The ground was wet and
so slippery the players were falling
constantly. Hugh Williamson was the
referee.
GOVERNOR DAVIS TAKES
UP BOB RICE'S FIGHT
Will Appeal to Legislature If Hot
Springs Club Refuses Horse
man a License.
LITTLE ROCK. Jan. B.âGovernor
Jefferson Davis has served notice on
the Hot Springs Jockey Club to the
effect that if Captain Bob Rice, out
lawed by the Western Jockey Club for
racing at St. Louis Union track last
year, is not permitted to race his
horses under the auspices of the Hot
Springs Club the matter will be taken
up with the Legislature. Davis wants
to see if something cannot be done to
rid the sport of horse racing in
sas from the influence and dictation of
the monopoly which arrogates to itself
the right to say when and where a
gentleman must race his own horse.
Governor Davis says he is determined
to see that Rice is honorably dealt
with in so far as the rules of the
Western Jockey Club operate in Ar
kansas.
ADAM ANDREW SELLING
STAKE NEXT IN LINE.
Rifling of Travers Calls for
General Adverse Criticism
â¦
JOCKEY BEATEN ON MANY FAVORITES
Good sport Is assured at Oakland this'
week, as an attractive programme is
offered. Wednesday there will be a
$1000 handicap for 3-year-olds and up
ward, at a mile and a sixteenth. The
same day the 2-year-olds will meet at
three and a half furlongs.
The Adam Andrew Selling stakes,
the feature of the week, has 116 eligi
bles. It will be run next Saturday-
The event will be worth more than
$3000, and is for 3-year-olds and over
at six and one-half furlongs. Among
the principal entries are Gold Money,
Military Man, Elliott, The Mighty,
Letola, Nigrette, Prince Brutus, Mar
tinmas, Sir Brillar, Pelham, Double
Six, Ishtar, Padua, Magrane, Hanault,
San Nicholas, Venator and Ananias.
There is also a mile handicap on
the programme for Saturday.
The criminally bad ride of Travers on '
Nigrette last Saturday was the general
theme of conversation among turfgoers |
yesterday, and the opinion was ex- j
pressed that the judges treated the
jockey very leniently. Eastern turfites i
say that the work of Travers in the
saddle at the Bennings meeting was I
extremely ragged and not above suspi- I
cion. Since December 9 Travers has I
met with defeat on the following pro
bounced favorites at Oakland: The j
Lieutenant, San Nicholas, Albemarle,
Venator, Handy Bill, Dr. Sherman,
Stillcho, Golden Buck, Briers on two
occasions, Sir Preston, Isabellita, Troy
twice, G. W. Trahern. Emma Reubold,
Red Cross Nurse, Honiton, Rector,
Mimo, Glendennlng, Best Man, Silver
skin and Nigrette. He also rode some
queer-looking races on second and
third choices, one especially on Aunt
Polly, when that mare was beaten out
of the show by Batidor. Forgetting the
day that Flying Torpedo downed G. W.
Trahern, the effort, if such it may be
termed, of Travers astride Nigrette on
Saturday was the limit. The idea of a
boy sitting still on a mare full of run 1
and only nodding, and a 100 to 1 Bhot ,
cutting out the pace. It all reads like .
a summer idyl of the bushes.
Aleck Ullman, a member of the
Metropolitan Turf Association, has ar
rived from New York.
TENNIS CRACKS
DODGE SHOWERS
Several Stars Hook Up in
Exciting Games of the
(ontinuous Tournament
The rain held off long enough yes
terday to allow most of the players to
complete their matches. A few of the
tardy ones were disappointed. In the
continuous tournament, three matches
were played. Carl Gardner, No. 9 in
the first class, beat George Busch, No.
8. and Melville Long, No. 12, beat
Chester Smith, No. 11. Moulten War
ner. No. 2 in the third class, beat Dr.
Lovegrove, No. 3.
Both of the first class matches were
v. on in straight sets. Gardner beat
Busch 6-4, 6-3 and Long beat Smith
6-1, 6-3. Busch was not in good condi
tion and his cleverer opponent was
never in danger of losing. Busch has
some good strokes but Is shy on en
durance. The Smith-Long match was
a walkover for the latter. Smith has
had no practice of late, while the fast
improving Long was at his best- Of
the three tournament maunes, the one
between the third class players, War
ner and Dr. Lovegrove, was by far the
closest. The doctor played his best
game and his younger opponent had to
extend himself to win. Some clever
practice games were piayed In doubles.
The best match was that in which
Albert Kenyon and Grant Smith beat
Harry Rolfe and Sidney Salisbury.
The later led in the early stages of
both sets but Kenyon and Smith fin
ished strongly and won out. The third
set stood 3-3 and deuce when the rain
put a stop to play. Following are the
results:

of the British steamship Emma, from

RUSSIAN WORKMEN DEMAND
TEN DOLLARS A MONTH
Fierce and Fatal Fighting in Connec
tion With Strike at Baku
Oil Fields.
BAKU, Jan. BâA big Are, presum
ably of incendiary origin, to-day de
stroyed sixteen tanks on the Nobels
camps and several adjoining prop
erties.
There has been no actual fighting
since January 5, when a collision oc
curred between strikers and Cossacks,
i and six workmen were killed and two
i Cossacks and thirty workmen injured.
! The strike has been in progress since
j December 26. The original dispute was
' over hours and wages. The first few
J days of the strike there was no
j trouble, but on December 29 crowds
threatened the guards stationed about
the refining works and Cossacks were
called out. Many men were injured in
desultory fighting between strikers and
soldiers at various points.
The wage question stih remains un
settled, the men demanding $10 to
$11.50 a month. A big meeting at which
jit was decided to continue the strike
j led up to the fighting on January 5.
RUNS ON SHOALS AT END
OF A SIX MONTHS' VOYAGE
Norwegian Lifeboat Damaged While
Entering the Harbor at Glou
cester, Mass.
GLOUCESTER, Mass., Jan. B.âThe
18-foot Norwegian lifeboat Uradd,
from Aalesund, Norway, which, after
an adventurous voyage of six months,
stranded in coming up the harbor
shortly after midnight to-day, was
floated this noon with only a small
hole In her outer skin. She was
towed to a local wharf and will prob
ably go to Boston for repairs.

LIVERPOOL, Jan. B.âThe Post
hears on good authority that J. W.
Lowther, M. P., will succeed Lord
Milner as Commissioner to South
Africa in April.

Anton Regains His Old Form.
Carlow Boy defeated Humboldt In
hollow fashion in the match race yes
terday at Union Coursing Park. The
Boy succeeded in winning the neces
sary three courses. Humboldt raised
the flag in the second trial, which was
the only course he scored. Corder's
dog showed the speed. In the first
three trials he led Humboldt from one
to three lengths, and in the last run I
he was five lengths in front of the son I
of Hot Stuff when the hare was forced
to. turn. Humboldt failed to score in
this course. Humboldt won his only
course by a point, He slightly out
worked the favorite in the second trial.
The talent laid against Humboldt in
the four races.
J. Sweeney's Richard Anton ran up
to his best form and captured the open
etake in championship style. He was
made one of the stake favorites in the
long-odds book. He showed the foot
in all his courses and worked the same
to perfection. In the final he raised
the flag over the Eastern doit. Cran
berry Sauce. The latter is now show
ing his true form, and will be a hard
one to beat from now on.
The defeat of McVey by Texas Ma
mie in the third round was a rude shock
to the wise ones. They freely laid 5 to
1 against Mamie, who won by outwork
ing the favorite in a limit course. Lost
Chord was bowled over in the first
round by The Raider, an outsider at 6
to 1. During the first twelve races
eight short ends scored at juicy odds.
The tide then turned, and from then on
but a few outsiders bothered the tal
ent. The hares were a strone lot and
provided excellent tests. Following are
the day's results, with the scores of
Judge P. J. Reilly:

PRICHARD HAS
HIGHEST SCORE
Shell Mound Marksmen
Toast Man Who Made the
Most Kings Last Season
A big crowd of marksmen turned out
at Shell Mound Park yesterday. The
weather was against good marksman
ship, as darkness and rain set in dur
ing the early afternoon and made it
almost impossible for the riflemen to
center their shots.
The main event of the day was the
wind-up shoot for the year of the
Golden Gate Rifle and Pistol Club.
Prizes to the successful marksmen
were distributed at the banquet given
in the large hall of the park. M. J.
White, president of the club, recounted
its successful history and introduced
W. C. Prichard, who won the honor of
king for the year 1904. Remarks were
made by many well-known marksmen.
In the re-entry match monthly con
test of the Golden Gate Club W. F.
; Blasse with 221 rings was high man.
In the pistol re-entry match M. J.
White with 89 was the leader, closely
followed by J. P. Cosgrave, with 83. 77
and 74 to his credit. The revolver con
test brought out good scores. W. C.
Prichard rolled up 95 out of a possible
100 and G. E. Frahm made 90. In the
annual contest Frank E. Mason was
the winner of the first prize, with 2290
rings, being nearly 23 rings out of a
possible 25, which is a very high score,
even among the champions. A. C.
Gehret was his nearest competitor,
with 2262 rings. The prize winners on
the pistol target were.W. F. Prichard.
945; G. E. Frahm, 944, and W. R. Proll.
944. The scores follow:

JENKINS IS AFTER TITLE
HELD BY JACK CARKEEK
Wrestling Giants Are Primed for a
Hard Struggle on Mat To
morrow Night.
The catch-as-catch-can champion
ship of the wrestling world is likely
to change hands to-morrow night at
Woodward's Pavilion when Tom Jen
kins goes after Jack Carkeek's title.
A visit to San Rafael yepterday re
vealed the fict that the Cleveland
giant is close to physical perfection
and confident of putting a new handle
on his name in the shape of a cham
pionship he has never claimed.
Jenkins wrestled with three Import
ed opponents and the way he tossed
the big fellows about was a good indi
cation of Just how good he felL Car
keek is slower to move than Jenkins
until he is on the mat Then his pent
up energy begins to exert itself and
the slow-going man in citizen's clothes
is a seething furnace of action. With
out being excitable or nervous, he is
ever ready to turn an opponent's mis
take to his advantage and loses no
time in doing it
