BAIN HAILED
AS CHAMPION
Invincible Al Hampton Is
Outplayed in a Special
Soft Ball Match Contest
Fred Bain defeated Al Hampton in
the special match game of handball
for a silver cup at the Olympic Club
yesterday afternoon. Bain's victory
stamps him as the best soft-handball
player on the coast. Hampton's de
feat was a surprise to the wise ones,
who lost considerable in betting. Bain
outplayed Hampton and won on his
merits. Hampton is a star hard ball
player, but cannot play soft ball with
such a fast man as Bain. Scores:
21-10, 21-8. Bain won the soft ball
tournament and the special cup put
up by Louis Rosenfeld and Kenneth
Rosenfeld.
Clarence Clough beat Robert Bain
in two straight games and C. Carr
beat Guy Ransome. W. P. Humphrey
and F. C. Wegener are to play next
Wednesday evening.
Entries for the interclub hard-ball
tournament to be given under the
auspices of the Pacific Amateur Ath
letic Association closed yesterday. The
games will be played at the Olympic,
St. Ignatius and Occidental clubs.
The Olympic club will be composed
of Louis Levy, Henry Cory, George
James and George Cunningham. J. J.
Gleason, substitute. Roger Cornell
will train the Olympic club team.
Each man must play twelve games.
Great interest is being taken in the
big tournament. A silver cup and in
dividual prizes will be awarded the
victorious team.

ANTIOCH. Jan. B.âThe Antioch

BUSY BURGLAR
VISITS STORES
Effects Entrance to Groceries
in the Western Addition
by Breaking Glass Doors
A busy burglar visited three gro
ceries in the Western Addition early
yesterday morning and was cought in
side the third. He entered in each
instance by breaking the glass door
and unlocking it by shoving his arm
through the hole-
Shortly before 2 o'clock Mrs. E.
Eaumback, 1601 Golden Gate avenue,
was awakened by the noise of breaking
â  glass. She looked out of her window
and saw a man entering the grocery
of Mrs. M. Hughes, 1534 Golden Gate
avenue. She telephoned to the O'Far
rell-street police station and Policemen
O'Brien and O'Neil were sent to the
grocery in the patrol wagon. They met
special officer Fianjjagan and took him
with them.
The front door was open and when
they entered they discovered a man
hiding behind the counter. He gave
his name as John Smith, a mill-hand,
21 years of age, and said he lived at 25
Powell street. When searched cigar
ettes, tobacco, sardines and matches
were found in his possession. He had
a loaded revolver and an opal pin.
He was arrested for burglary.
The police were later notified that
the grocery of John Rauch, 1601 Turk
street, had been entered by a burglar
by breaking the glass in the door. An
opal pin and $3.36 had been stolen from
the cash register. Conrad Rlchter,
ICO2 Turk street, identified the opal pin
found on Smith as one he had given to
Rauch for safe keeping.
About 1.15 a. m- Sergeant Boyd and
Special Policeman Doyle noticed that
the glass in the side door of the
grocery of I* M. Walter, California and
Devisadern streets, had been broken.
They notified the proprietor, who lives
opposite, but after making an exam
ination he said that nothing had been
stolen. The police are satisfied that
v Smith did this job also.
Tenant and Landlord Fight.
Milton P. Seeley of 117 Franklin
street and Henry Baseling, proprietor
of the flat which Seeley occupies, en
gaged in a fight last night which end
ed by both being arrested. Seeley waa
charged with assault with a deadly
weapon, Baseling with battery. Basel
ing went to Seeley's house to collect
the rent, which, it is said, was long
overdue. Seeley refused to make a
settlement and thereupon Baseling en
deavored to eject his tenant from the
premises. In the fight that ensued it
is alleged that Seeley struck Baseling
over the head with a chisel.
SEEKS REFUGE
WITH FRIENDS
Pretty Margaret Seals Is
Discovered by Police and
Is Being Kept in Prison
BLAMES HER PARENTS
Says Her Mother Compelled
Her to Accept Annoying
Attentions of Employer
Margaret Seals, the pretty girl whose
disappearance was reported to the po
lice on Saturday, Is now In the ma
tron's room at the City Prison, aa she
does not want to return to her parents.
Captain Burnett will endeavor to get
a suitable position for the girl, as he Is
Impressed with the truthfulness of her
story as to her unhappy surroundings.
She will be asked this morning to swear
to a complaint charging D. Liapis,
fruit dealer, 254 Fourth street, her for
mer employer, with battery.
The girl said that after leaving the
fruit store on Friday afternoon she
bought a few things with the $2 she got
from Liapis and went to the house of
Mrs. Dillingham, 1278 Howard street,
where she and her parents formerly
lived. She remained there till yester
day morning. When Mrs. Dillingham
saw from the morning papers that the
police were searching for the girl she
telephoned to the station. Detective
Taylor went for the girl and took her to
headquarters. Mrs. Dillingham corrob
drated the girl's story.
The girl said that twice before she
had left Liapis' store, but her mother
compelled her to go back, although she
told her of how Liapis annoyed her and
wanted to kiss her. Liapis told her
mother that he would marry the girl,
but she did not like him. She will be
15 years old in June. She tried on Sat
urday to get a position in a Mission
street store, but failed. She has been
working for about three years. Her
mother, she says, has often beaten her
and she has no desire to see either of
her parents again. Both are, she says,
too fond of liquor and her mother used
to drink with sU"ange men when her
father was away from home. She de
nied that she had ever met any young
man clandestinely or otherwise.
Rich Woman Lives as Miser.
Mrs. Polina Cole, about 65 years old
and the owner of several buildings
occupied as flats on Howard street, be
tween Third and Fourth, was found
in a basement at 1036 Howard street
yesterday forenoon suffering from
cerebral hemorrhage. She was re
moved to the Emergency Hospital,
where Dr. (). S. Kucich gave her
treatment. The basement where she
was found was very filthy, and the
woman, too, was so unclean as to be
loathsome in appearance. Relatives of
this woman miser say she is so penuÂ»
rious as to prefer sleeping on a dirty
mat on the floor rather than pay for
washing the bed linen. After a liberal
ablution at the Emergency Hospital
she was transferred by her relatives
to the German Hospital.
An Atcnison doctor says, comment
ing on the us-ual good health of the
ptuple: 'There is nothing doing in
Aichison, except a little practice on
sick doctors."

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, MONDAY, JANUARY 9, 1905.
LIVES CLOSED
BY GAS FUMES
James Hickey, Old Park La
borer, Found DeadâPo
liceman Has Narrow Escape
JET PARTLY TURNED ON
Y. Asakawa, a Japanese Ser
vant, Who Went to Bed HI,
Passes Away by Monoxide
Two cases of gas asphyxiation were
reported to the Morgue yesterday,
both supposed to have been the result
of accident. The first was that of
James Hickey, 1185 Waller street. He
was 67 years of age and for the laat
twenty-seven years had been employed
as a laborer at Golden Gate Park.
Hickey went to bed about 9 o'clock
on Saturday night in excellent spirits.
Shortly after 6 o'clock yesterday
morning his daughter, Annie, detected
the odor of gas and traced it to a
room occupied by Policeman W. L.
Ceinar. The gas Jet was all right In
his room. She awakened him with
considerable difficulty and it was some
time before he was able to stagger to
his feet.
She left Ceinar and entered her
father's room. He was unconscious in
bed. Ceinar notified the Park police
station and Hickey was taken to the
Park Emergency Hospital, where life
was pronounced extinct. The body
was not taken to the Morgue.
The door of Hickey's room was
open a few inches and the window was
also slightly open. He was in the
habit of leaving a glass of wine at his
bedside to take during the night and
it is supposed he turned on the gas
to drink the wine, as the glass was
empty. The jet was partly open.
The other case was that of Y. Asa
kawa, a Japanese servant in the
employ of Mrs. Andrew J. Moul
der, 1941 Clay street. When he re
tired on Saturday night he' com
plained of being sick. As he did
not appear at his usual hour yesterday
morning Mrs. Moulder thought she
would allow him to sleep till she re
turned at noon from church. When
she returned from church she went to
his room* and.found him dead in bed.
Gas was flowing from an open Jet.

Drinks Acid Over Lost Lover.
After having a quarrel in the back
room of a saloon on Eighth and Fol
som streets last night Celia Cosbie, a
ycung woman, living at 337 Ninth
street, drank carbolic acid because her
friend, G. W. Parsons, refused to take
her out. The woman's act was quick
ly discovered and she was sent to the
Central Emergency Hospital for treat
ment. She is expected to recover.
Falls on a Knife.
Tom Chirm, a Chinese, employed at
the McAllister Hotel, was yesterday
cut on the thigh by falling from a
table and striking on a knife, which
was lying on the floor. He was treated
at the Central Emergency Hospital.
FINDS POISON
IN HER COFFEE
Gustay Ahlstedt Accused
by Wife of Having Had
Desire to Take Her Life
HE ONCE SHOT A MAN
Police Think They Have a
Good Case Against Pris
oner in Their Custody
For the second time in three years
Gusto v Ahlstedt, twice married, is In
custody of the police charged with a
serious crime. He is accused by his
wife, Margaret, with having attempted
to poison her with strychnine. The
woman says that last Thursday her
husband, from whom she Is expecting
to get a divorce, entered her rooms at
331 Jessie street and placed poison in
some Jellies and also in a coffee pot
that was on the stove.
Mr*. Ahlstedt, on entering the prem
ises, discovered an empty bottle in
which had been the poison. Remem
bering that on January 29, 190?, Ahl
stedt had killed a man whom he ac
cused of winning the affections of his
first wife, the second Mrs. Ahlstedt
took time and her designing spouse by
the forelock. Soon thereafter Police
man Thomas Finley landed hubby in
Jail.
The. charge'against Ahlstedt is vio
lating section 347 of the Penal Code,
which provides punishment for poison
ers. Ahlstedt denies that he attempted
to commit a crime and declares that
he knows nothing about the poison
that is said to have been injected into
his wife's eatables.
Ahlstedfs first wife died about two
years ago and soon afterward he mar
ried the woman who now wants him
sent to San Quentin for conspiracy.
Divorce proceedings have been pend
ing, but, according to Mrs. Ahlstedfs
story, the man has been attempting
to effect a reconciliation. She says
that on last Thursday, during her ab
sence from home, Ahlstedt left his
rooming place at 55 East Park street
and at the Jessie street house gained
an entrance through a back window,
that he dosed the jellies and the cof
fee and then dropped the bottle that
had contained the poison. It was
found on the floor. Mrs. Ahlstedt re
ported the matter to the police and
Detective Gibson was detailed to make
an investigation. Gibson thinks he can
prove that Al&stedt bought the poison
and that he afterward transferred it to
a bottle of a particular make that was
known to have been some time in his
possession.
On January 29, 1902, Ahlstedt shot
and killed Thomas Normile a laborer.
Normile and Mrs. Ahlstedt had been
visiting friends near the Ahlstedt home
on Bernai Heights and both returned
at a late hour intoxicated. Ahlstedt
found his wife and Normile sitting to
gether in the backyard of his home and
he shot and instantly killed the man.
He was acquitted of the charge of
murder.
Ahlstedt has been addicted to the
use of liquor for years. He is a labor
er, employed occasionally in the street
department.
An Atchison man is so busy that he
could hardly find time to attend hiy
own funeral, to say nothing of attend
ing the funerals of friends. 3

SALOON VISITED
BY MASKED MEN
John Bielenberg, Proprietor,
and George Goodwin Look
Into Muzzle of Revolvers
John Bielenberg, saloon-keeper, 398
Ninth street, was chatting- with George
Goodwin of 1492 Folsom street about
1:30 o'clock yesterday morning when
two men wearing red colored handker
chiefs over their faces entered. Each
had a revolver in his right hand and
{he taller of the two ordered Bielenberg
and Goodwin to throw up their hands.
The order was quickly obeyed. One
kept the muzzle of a revolver pointed
toward the victims, while the other
deftly went through their pockets. A
silver watch valued at $30, a gold link
chain valued at $40 and $3 were taken
from Blelenberg's pockets. Goodwin
lost a gold watch valued at $25, a gold
plated chain with gold padlock and $2.
The cash register was then opened and
$10 taken from it
"If you make a noise after we leave,"
said the taller of the two robbers, "we
will come back and kill you." They
hurried from the place and no sooner
had they disappeared than Bielenberg
blew his police whistle long and loudly.
Policemen Hammond and Madden
heard the whistle and ran toward the
saloon. They were told of the hold-up
and made a search of the neighborhood,
but could find no trace of the men. De
tective Mulcahy had occasion to visit
the saloon about five minutes after the
hold-up.
From the coolness displayed by the
robbers it is thought they are old hands
at the business. One is described as
about 30 years of age. 5 feet 10 inches
tall, and the other about 5 feet 8 inches
tall. His handkerchief dropped from
his face for a moment while he was
going through Blelenberg's pockets and
he had a smooth, red face. Bielenberg
thinks he would know the man.
HEBREW HOME FOR AGED
ELECTS YEAR'S OFFICERS
Late Reports Show That the Condition
of the Association Is One
of Prosperity.
The annual meeting of the members
of the association formed for conduct
ing the Hebrew Home for Aged Dis
abled was held yesterday afternoon
in the main room of the home, at 507
Lombard street.
The meeting was for the purpose of
electing a board of directors for the
year and hearing reports from the va
rious officers and committees on work
performed by the association during
the: past year. The report of the pres%
ident thoroughly reviewed the needs
attended to by the society, and the
treasurer's report showed that the
home is in good condition financially.
The following officers and directors
were elected for 1905: President, S.
Polack; vice president, P. Abraham
son; secretary, Mrs. Kaskell; treasurer,
P. Fabian, and directors. M. Bender,
E. E. Kahn, L Moss, E. Levy and Miss
A. Levy.

Persian Speaks at Y. M. C. A.
"Persia, Its People, Customs and
Religions," was the topic of an inter
esting lecture delivered yesterday af
ternoon at the Y. M. C. A. Auditorium
by Joseph Koshaba of Oroomiah, Per
sia. The speaker is preparing to re
turn to his native land after having
studied in American colleges for the
last eight years. He has taken a
course of medic'ne and surgery at the
College of Physicians and Surgeons,
Minneapolis, Minn.

THEATER-GOERS'
OLD FAVORITE
DIES DESTITUTE
From the glare of the footlights, the
applause of thousands of old San Fran
ciscans, and the portrayal of leading
roles with great actors of by-gone
years, to a slab at the Morgue is the
sad life's end of the brilliant and tal
ented Nellie Cummings.
For many years Nellie Cummings.
as leading woman for John McCullough
and Lawrence Barrett at the old Cali
fornia Theater, in the truly palmy days
of the drama, will be remembered by
old-time playgoers. After that time
she drifted East, playing with many
first-class companies, notably those of
Richard Mansfield. Frederick Warde
and Edna Wallace Hopper.
With the approach of age. engage
ments came less frequently and she
contracted asthma, which finally
caused her death yesterday among
sympathetic strangers at 418 Kearny
street. She had been playing a brief
engagement with a small stock com
pany In San Jose, but returned two
days ago, worn and weak, seeking a
place to die. She had many friends
here, from whom she withheld the
knowledge of her reduced finances and
misery.
Miss Cummings came from Boston.
Mass., of a fine "Backbay" family, and
married a man in private life. At one
time she owned valuable real estate on
ADVERTISEMENrS. _ - _, L - -
j Magnin's
i Clearance \

1 Commences IfjP \ I
Wabash avenue, Chicago. She num
bered among her friends in this city
Judge D. W. Murphy. Dr. Orlof N. Or
low, Mrs. Sarah Forbes 1 and many of
the older generation of actors and act
resses.
HOES SUFFERING ENDS.
Richard Derby Passes Away After a
Long Illness.
The prolonged illness of Richard
Derby, for many years secretary of the
Pacific Improvement Company and
one of the best known men in Central
California, ended in the unfortunate
man's death Saturday at his home in
Redwood City. He had been sick for
several months and the end waa not
unexpected by the members of his
family and the attending physicians.
The funeral took place yesterday,
the place of interment being Cypress
Lawn Cemetery, where many friend*
gathered to pay their respects to tha
dead.
